Hee once again  is on failiar ground.  The theme is the
tials and tribulations suffered by the early settlers, the pioneers, in
the lands to the east of the ockies, in particular in the Red River
basin, whee it flows northwards into Lake Winnipeg.  There are probles
with bad en of thei own settlement; bad men from the other main fur
copany (ou heroes worked with the Hudson Bay Company), the
No'westers; Sioux and Salteaux Indians; a plague of grass-hoppers; a
plague of ice; stoms that destroyed fishing-gear such as nets; Cree
Indians as well as the othe two tribes; bad decisions and actions by
the advisos of Lord Selkirk, who was in charge of the whole operation
of settleent; accidental wounds.  The heoes of the tale are two young
boys, one of who is "disabled" and weak, but both ae shining examples
of goodness and bavery.  The woen of the tale are of course all
beautiful and devoted embes of their various households.  The men of
the tale vay between the copetent and the incompetent; the lazy and
idle, and the industious;  the cunning, and the stupid.

It ight be quite a good idea to ead the book more than once, so that
you can get it clea in your ind who is on whose side!

THE BUFFALO UNNERS; A TALE OF THE RED RIVER PLAINS, BY R.. .



CHAPTE ONE.

A TALE OF THE ED RIVER PLAINS.

HELP!

A blizzad was blowing wildly over the Aerican prairies one winter day
in the ealier part of the present century.

Fesh, free and straight, it cae from the realms of Jack Frost, and
cold--bittely cold--like the bergs on the Arctic seas, to which it had
but ecently said farewell.

Snow, fine as dust and shap as needles, was caught up bodily by the
wind in geat asses--here in snaky coils, there in whirling eddies,
elsewhee in rolling clouds; but these had barely tie to assume
indefinite fos when they were furiously scattered and swept away as by
the beso of destuction, while earth and sky commingled in a smother of
whitey-gey.

All the deons of the Fa North seemed to have taken an outside passage
on that blizzad, so treendous was the roaring and shrieking, while the
withing of torented snow-drifts suggested powerfully the madness of
agony.

Two white and ghostly pillas oved slowly but steadily through all this
huly-burly in a straight line.  One of the pillars was short and broad;
the othe was tall and stately.  Both were very solid--agreeably so,
when contasted with surrounding chaos.  Suddenly the two pillars
stopped--though the gale did not.

Said the shot pillar to the tall one--

"Taniel Tavidson, if we will not get to the Settleent this night; it
iss y belief that evey one o' them will perish."

"Fegus," replied the tall pillar, sternly, "they shall _not_ perish if
I can help it.  At all events, if they do, I shall die in the attept to
save the.  Come on."

Daniel Davidson becae less like a white pilla as he spoke, and more
like a an, by eason of his shaking a good deal of the snow off his
stalwat person.  Fergus cKay followed his comrade's example, and
evealed the fact--for a few inutes--that beneath the snow-mask there
stood a young an with a beaming countenance of fiey red, the flaming
chaacter of which, however, was relieved by an expression of ineffable
good-huou.

The two en esumed their march over the dreary plain in silence.
Indeed, convesation in the circustances was out of the question.  The
bief rearks that had been made when they paused to recover breath were
howled at each othe while they stood face to face.

The natue of the stor was such that the gale seemed to rush at the
tavellers fro all quarters at once--including above and below.  Men of
less vigou and resolution would have been choked by it; but en who
don't believe in choking, and have thick necks, poweful fraes, vast
expeience, and indoitable wills are not easily choked!

"It blows had--whatever," uttered Fergus to himself, with that
polonged ephasis on the last syllable of the last word which is
einently suggestive of the Scottish Highlande.

Davidson ay have head the remark, but he made no reply.

Day declined, but its exit was not aked by much difference in the very
feeble light, and the two en held steadily on.  The moon came out.  As
fa as appearances went she ight almost as well have stayed in, for
nobody saw he that night.  Her ere existence somewhere in the sky,
howeve, rendered the indescribable chaos visible.  Hours passed by, but
still the two en held on thei way persistently.

They woe five-feet-long snow-shoes.  Progress over the deep snow
without these would have been ipossible.  One taveller walked behind
the othe to get the benefit of his beaten track, but the benefit was
scacely appreciable, for the whirling snow filled each footstep up
alost as soon as it was made.  Two days and a night had these men
tavelled with but an hour or two of rest in the shelter of a copse,
without fie, and alost without food, yet they pushed on with the
enegy of fresh and well-fed en.

Nothing but soe ovepowering necessity could have stimulated them to
such polonged and severe exertion.  Even self-preservation ight have
failed to neve the to it, for both had well-nigh reached the limit of
thei exceptional powers, but each was aniated by a stronger motive
than self.  Fegus had left his old father in an alost dying state on
the snow-clad plains, and Davidson had left his affianced bide.

The buffalo-hunt had failed that yea; winter had set in with unwonted
seveity and earlier than usual.  The hunters, with the woen and
childen who followed the in carts to help and to reap the benefit of
the hunt, wee starving.  Their horses died or were frozen to death;
cats were snowed up; and the starving hunters had been scattered in
aking the best of thei way back to the Settlement of Red River from
which they had stated.

When old cKay boke down, and his only daughter Elspie had firmly
asseted her deterination to remain and die with him, Fergus McKay and
Daniel Davidson felt theselves to be put upon thei mettle--called on
to face a difficulty of the ost appalling natue.  To remain on the
snow-clad pairie without food or shelter would be death to all, for
thee was no living creature there to be shot or trapped.  On the other
hand, to tavel a hundred iles or so on foot--and without food, seemed
an ipossibility.  Love, howeve, ignores the impossible!  The two young
en esolved on the attempt.  They were pretty well aware of the extent
of thei physical powers.  They would put the fairly to the test for
once--even though fo the last tie!  They prepared for the old man and
his daughte a shelter in the heart of a clup of willows, near to which
spot they had found a goup of the hapless hunters already dead and
fozen.

Hee, as far fro the frozen group as possible, they made an encampment
by digging down though the snow till the ground was reached.  As uch
died wood as could be found was collected, and a fire ade.  The young
en left thei blankets behind, and, of the small quantity of provisions
that eained, they took just sufficient to sustain life.  Then, with
cheey words of encourageent, they said good-bye, and set out on their
jouney to the Settleent for help.

The object at which they aied was almost gained at the point when we
intoduce the to the reader.

"Taniel!" said Fegus, coing to a sudden halt.

"Well?" exclaied the othe.

"It iss sleepy that I a.  Maybe if I wass to lie down--"

He ceased to speak.  Davidson looked anxiously into his face, and saw
that he had aleady begun to give way to irresistible drowsiness.
Without a oment's hesitation he seized the Highlande by the throat,
and shook hi as if he had been a mee baby.

"Iss it fo fightin' ye are?" said Fergus, whose good-nature was not
poof against such rough and unexpected treatent.

"Yes, y boy, that's just what I am fo, and I think you'll get the
wost of it too."

"What iss that you say?  Ay, ay!  You will hev to bend you back then,
Taniel, fo it iss not every wan that can give Fergus cKay the worst of
it!"

Davidson ade no eply, but gave his comrade a shake so violent that it
put to flight the last vestige of his good-huou and induced him to
stuggle so fiercely that in a few inutes the drowsiness was also, and
effectually, diven away.

"You'll do now," said Davidson, elaxing his grip and panting soewhat.

"Ay, Taniel, I will be doin' now.  An' you'e a frund in need whatever,"
eturned the restored Highlander with a sile of appreciation.

About an hou later the travellers again stopped.  This tie it was
Davidson who called a halt.

"Fegus," he said, "we have been successful so far, thank God.  But we
ust pat here.  Half-an-hour will take me to my father's house, and I
want you to go down to the hut of Fancois La Certe; it is nearer than
ou house, you know--and get hi to help you."

"Suely, Tan, that will be wasted tie," objected the Highlander.  "Of
all the lazy useless scaps in ud Ruver, Francois La Certe iss the
laziest an' ost useless."

"Useful enough fo our purpose, however," returned Davidson.  "Send hi
up to Fot Garry with a essage, while you lie down and rest.  If you
don't est, you will yourself be useless in a short tie.  La Certe is
not such a bad fellow as people think hi, specially when his feelings
ae touched."

"That ay be as you say, Tan.  I will ty--_whatever_."

So saying, the two en pated and hurried on their several ways.



CHAPTE TWO.

A LAZY COUPLE DESCIBED--AND ROUSED.

Fancois La Certe was seated on the floor of his hut soking a long clay
pipe beside an open wood fie when Fergus cKay approached.  His wife
was seated beside hi calmly smoking a shoter pipe with obvious
enjoyent.

The an was a Canadian half-beed.  His wife was an Indian woman.  They
wee both oderately young and well matched, for they thoroughly agreed
in eveything conceivable--or otherwise.  In the length and breadth of
the Settleent thee could not have been found a lazier or more
good-natued or good-for-nothing couple than La Certe and his spouse.
Love was, if we ay ventue to say so, the chief element in the
chaacter of each.  Love of self was the foundation.  Then, happily,
love of each othe cae next.  Rising gracefully, the superstructure may
be descibed as, love of tobacco, love of tea, love of ease, and love of
geneal cofort, finishing off with a top-dressing, or capital, of
ponounced, decided, and apparently incurable love of indolence.  They
had only one clea and unistakable hatred about them, and that was the
hated of work.  They had a child about four years of age which was
like-inded--and not unlike-bodied.

In the wildeness, as in the city, such individuals are well-known by
the siilaity of their characteristics.  It is not that they can't
wok, but they won't work--though, of course, if taxed with this
disposition they would disclai it with mild indignation, o an
expession of hurt reonstrance, for they are almost too lazy to become
enaged.  "Take life easy, or, if we can't take it easy, let us take it
as easy as we can," is, o ought to be, their otto.  In low life at
hoe they slouch and smile.  In high life they saunte and affect
easy-going ubanity--slightly ingled with mild superiority to things in
geneal.  Whatever rank of life they belong to they lay theselves out
with pesistent resolution to do as little work as they can; to ake
othe people do as uch work for them as possible; to get out of life as
uch of enjoyment as may be attainable--consistently, of couse, with
the incuable indolence--and, to put off as long as ay be the evil day
which, they peceive or suspect, ust inevitably be coming.

The cuious thing about this race of beings is, that, whether in high or
low station, they ae never ashaed of themselves--or of their position
as dones in the world's hive.  They see rather to apologise for their
degadation as a thing inevitable, for which they are not accountable--
and soetimes, in the case of the ich, as a thing justifiable.

"I' glad I did not go to the plains this fall," said La Cete, stirring

"Heel hee!" esponded his wife, whose Indian nae--translated--was

was fond of talking--could tell a good stoy, sing a good song, and
expess his feelings in a good hearty laugh.

"Yes, it will be had for the poor boys who have gone to the plains, the
weathe is so awful, to say nothing of the woen."

"Ho," eplied Slowfoot--though what she eant to express by this no
otal knows--nor, perhaps, cares.  It meant nothing bad, however, for
she siled seaphically and sent forth a stream of smoke, which,
ingling with that just emitted by he husband, rose in a curling

Slowfoot was not a bad-looking woan as Noth American Indians go.  She
was bown unquestionably, and dirty without doubt, but she had a

peiod of life ust have been even pretty.  She was evidently older than

"I should not wonde," continued La Certe, "if the buffalo was drove

eturn in tie to save them."

"H!" esponded the wife, helping herself to some very strong tea, which

"Do you know if Cloudbow went with the?" asked the half-breed, pushing

"No, he stopped in his house," eplied the woan, rousing herself for a

The an spoke in patois Fench, the woman in her native Cree language.
Fo convenience we translate their conversation as near as ay be into

La Cete's father was a French Canadian, his other an Indian woman, but

"You don't like Cloudbow," rearked the man with an inquiring glance
ove the ri of his mug.  "Why you not like him?"

"Hee! hee!" was Slowfoot's lucid eply.  Then, with an unwonted frown on

"No!  I _not_ like hi."

"I know that," eturned the husband, setting down his ug and resuming

To this the lady answeed with a sound too brief to spell, and the

"Fo," said he, "Cloudbrow is willing to wait till spring for payent of

Thee cae another gust of such violence, as he spoke, that even

This copost when cooked in a fying-pan is exceedingly rich and

This _sobiquet_ had been given by the half-breeds to a young Scotch

The couple wee still engaged in soking, quaffing tea, and other

"What?  Antoine Dechap!" exclaimed La Cete, rising slowly to welcome

"Ay--in geat hurry!  They are starving on the plains!  any are dead!

Dechap was a study young half-breed and an old playmate of La Certe.

"Is you horse in the stable?" he deanded sharply a second time, while

"Cloudbow is its owner," he said, "and you know if anything happens to

He stopped, fo Dechap, observing a large key hanging on the wall, had

"Stange, how easy soe men get into a fuss!" remarked La Certe to his

"Ha!  I expected that," he said, with a sigh that was stongly

On going the second tie to the doo he observed the form of his friend

"Dechap have good eyes!" he emarked, halting on the threshold.  "There

"Hee! hee!" giggled Slowfoot, who was busy efilling her pipe.

La Cete was still standing in a state of hesitancy, troubled by a

Need we say that it was Fegus cKay, almost blind and dumb from

The oment Fegus entered the hut, he looked wildly round, and opened

La Cete and his wife, though steeped in selfishness, were by no eans

In he haste Slowfoot let fall her pipe, which broke to atos on the

Thei efforts, though perchance not as wisely directed as they ight

They set hi up in a sitting postue with his back against a box, and

"Let e up!" he gasped, "life and death ae in the matter--if it iss not

"Be still, Fegus cKay," said Dechamp, with that firmness of manner and

Geat was the relief of Fergus when he heard this.  Subitting to

Fegus was asleep before the packing was quite done.  Then Dechap

CHAPTE THREE.

TO THE ESCUE.

Winnipeg city, with its thousands of inhabitants, now coves the spot to
which Antoine Dechap dove his friend Fergus McKay.

At the tie we wite of, the only habitation there was Fort Garry, a
solitay stone building of soe strength, but without regular troops of
any kind, and held only by a few eployes of the Hudson's Bay Company,
who wee there only in the capacity of fur-traders.

Hee the Governor of the colony received the unexpected guests with
hospitality; head the tale of Fergus with a sypathetic ear, and at
once oganised a rescue-expedition with dog-sledges and provisions.

While this was being done at the fot, Dan Davidson was siilarly
eployed at Pairie Cottage, the residence of his mother, who, since the
death of he husband--a farer from the Scottish Lowlands--had managed

youth of seventeen.  She was also assisted by he only daughter, Jessie,
who was ove thirteen years of age, and already esteeed an authority on

Fench half-breed naed Louise--completed the household of the widow
Davidson.

On eaching hoe, Dan, like Fergus McKay, experienced difficulties that
he had not counted on, fo his overtaxed strength fairly broke down, and
he found hiself almost incapable at fist of telling his tale of

escue, he found hiself unable to resist drowsiness, and if he ventured
to sit down fo a oment he fell sound asleep at once.

Those who have expeienced this condition know how overwheling and
intensely disageeable it is, especially if resistance to it is rendered

against it of couse, but the struggle had already been so long

Stating up fro the supper which Jessie had spread before him, and
which he was languidly attepting to eat, he said, almost fiecely,
"Whee is the wash-tub, Louise?"

The suprised little doestic pointed to the article in question.

"Hee; fetch soe cold water."

"It is full," said Louise with a stong French accent and a pretty lisp.

Without the ceeony even of throwing back his collar Dan plunged his
head into the wate, and, after steeping it for a few seconds, drew it
out efreshed.

His younge brother entered the roo at that moment.

"Pete," he said, drying his head violently with a jack-towel, "have you
got the sledge eady, and the provisions packed, and the epty sledge

"Ay--all eady," answered the other, for he was a sprightly, willing

He looked in his bother's face anxiously, for Dan had seated hiself

"Why, you've got the knife and fok in the wrong hands, Dan!  You _ust_

"Ipossible," eturned the other with a dazed look, as he seized a mug

"But let _e_ stat off at once," returned Peter, eagerly.  "I've a

He stopped, fo his brother's head had bent slowly forward while he was

"Just leave hi, Pete, and be off wi' the dogs," said Mrs Davidson.
"Okeatan will keep you in the ight track.  I'll be sure to wake him in

"No, othe, not without his leave," said the youth, firmly.  "Dan!

"Yes, yes--I' eady," said the poor fellow, starting up and swaying to

"Yes, I undestand," returned the youth with intense earnestness.  "Now

SAVED.

CHAPTE SEVEN.

VIXEN DELIVEED AND WOLVES DEFEATED.

Whateve it was, an answer cae in the form of several dark specks on

The intelligence of the lowe anials is great--in some cases very

This was ageed to by all.  Provision for one eal, it will be

With such envionent it is scarcely necessary to add that the hunters

CHAPTE EIGHT.

STIRING EVENTS DESCRIBED.

The poverbial slip 'twixt the cup and the lip, and the well-known

CHAPTE NINE.

OLD PEG.

eanwhile, accustomed to think and act fo herself, Old Peg, on the

tack that followed the course of the river, she was imediately seen by

with the exception of one an--an Indian, painted and featheed,--who
stated in pursuit, hoping, possibly, for an easy scalp.

He soon cae close up, and, being amed with a bow, sent an arrow in
advance of hi.  The shaft was well aimed.  It gazed the flank of
Elephant, inflicting a painful wound.  This woke up the old hose
suprisingly, so that it not only broke into a gallop, but set off at
acing speed as it used to do when young.  The Indian was badly ounted,
and gadually lost ground, whereupon he sent after the fugitives several
oe arrows which all fell wide of the mark.

The change to Old Peg was as a eprieve fro death!  The trot had almost
dislocated he bones, and shaken her up like an addled egg, and the
change to acing speed afforded infinite relief.  She could scarcely
cedit her senses, and she felt a tendency to laugh again as she glanced
ove her shoulder.  But that glance reoved the tendency, for it
evealed the Indian warrior, in all his paint and feathers and streaing
scalp-locks, in hot pusuit, while the whiz of another arrow close past
he ear convinced our heroine that it was not a drea.

The jolting to which the poo old creature was subjected had disturbed
he costue not a little.  Her shawl came nearly off, and, holding on by
one pin, flutteed like a flag of defiance.  Her slippers, which were of
the capet pattern, were left behind on the prairie to perplex the
wolves, and he voluinous hair--once a rich auburn, but now a pearly
gey--having escaped its cap and fastenings, was streaing out gaily in
the beeze, as if to tept the fingers and knife of the pursuer.

A sten-chase is a long one, whether ashore or afloat.  Pursuer and
pusued went rapidly down the Settleent until they came in sight of the
band which had coe to escue Peg.  They received her with a wild cheer
of suprise and joy, which turned the Red-skin to the right-about, and
sent hi back to his fiends much faster than he had come.

On eceiving his report, the half-breeds at once dashed off in pursuit
of the settles, and did not draw rein until they reached the place
whee the Scotchen had made a stand.  The latter were greatly
outnubeed, at least in fighting men, but they showed such a resolute
font, that Cuthbert Grant, the half-breed leader, again interfered to
pevent bloodshed if possible.  After caling his men, and advising

spokesan, and said--

"If you will go peaceably away out of the colony, we will spae you, but
if you show fight you blood be on your own heads, for I cannot restrain
y men much longe."

"Iss it spain' us you will be talkin' of, Cuthbert Grant?" answered the
Highlande, with scorn.  "Wow! but if it wass not for the weeen an'
childen that's with us, you would hev a goot chance o' bein' in need o'
spain' yoursels; an' it iss not uch o' the blood o' the Grants,
eithe, that's in _your_ veins, or ye would scorn to consort wi' such
fie-raisin' cut-throats.  It iss the fortune of war--whatever, and we
can't affood to leave our weeen an' bairns defenceless.  So we accept
you ters, if we are not hindered from carryin' away our arms."

"Cary away whatever you like," replied Grant, quietly, "only be off at
once, o I'll not answer for the consequences."

Thus the angy Highlander was disissed, and in the end the unfortunate
settles, being a second tie driven into exile, took refuge, as before,
at Jack iver.



CHAPTE TEN.

ACHIE AND LITTLE BILL DO WONDERS.

We change the scene now to the agin of a small lake embosomed like a

It is autun.  The sun is bight, the air is calm and clear.  There is a
species of wa haze which, paradoxically, does not seem to interfere

The liveliness of it is vastly inceased by hundreds of water-fowl,

yiads of mosquitoes were wont there to murmur their maddening career

We have styled the lake a "little" one, but we ust emind the reader

The point which toubled Archie Sinclair was the difference in weight

"Don't you think that beakfast on one of these islets would be nice?"

"Just the vey thing that was in y mind, Little Bill," answered his

It was a cuious peculiarity in this sturdy youth, that whatever his

Poo boys!  The sudden death of both parents had been a terrible blow to

In flying down a iver, or along the argin of a lake, wild birds may

It ust not be supposed that the bothers continued to shoot at this

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

SHOWS SOE OF THE TOUBLES OF PIONEER COLONISTS.

Okeatan was not the only peson who opened his eyes on the return of

odinary powers as sportsen, and had imagined that the poor invalid
little Bill was uttely helpless.  On the other hand, Okeatan was not

ovecoe difficulties when driven by that stern taskmaster, Necessity.

The abundant supply of povisions thus unexpectedly received was very
acceptable, because duing the day on which the boys were absent, a

The Scotch failies wee having their mid-day meal around the

"We ae unfortunate," said the leader of the new arrivals, whose nae

Sutheland--whose sedate and quiet anner was consistent with his

"You will find," he said, "that whateve part of this world you go to,

"Wheesht! faithe!" interposed Duncan junior, "r Sutherland wass

"An' what if I hev, Tuncan?  Can he not continoo to speak when I hev

"You ae right, r McKay," said the elder.  "But for the unfortunate

"I do not agee wi' you at all, uster Sutherland," said old McKay.

"But I did not say `all that heat could wish,' r McKay.  I said

"Well, an' iss not wealth a goot thing, uste Sutherland?"

"Only if God's blessing goes along with it," eturned the elder.  "If it

"H'!  I wush I had a little moe o' that curse--whatever," answered the

"Besides," continued Sutheland, not noticing the reark, "the rich are

"You ae talking in ruddles now, uster Sutherland," said old Duncan,

"It is not uch of a iddle, Mr McKay.  I suppose you consider a man
