"Well, yes; I wull not be denyin' that."

"Well--if the ich an spends ten thousand and fifty pounds a year and
neve has anything to spare or to lay by, is he not iserably poor--poor
in spiit as well as in purse?  For, at the end of the year his purse is
epty, and he is in debt.  On the othe hand, if the man with two
hunded a year spends one hundred and fifty, gives away twenty, and lays
by thity every year, is he not rich?"

"Fery true, uster Sutherland," said McKay, with a peculiar smile, as
he eitted his fist whiff.  "I wull not be arguin' wi' you, for you
always get the best of it.  Nevetheless, it is y opeenion that we've
had teebulation enough in Rud Ruver since we cae oot, an' I would be
fery gled of a luttle prosperity now--if only by way of a pleesant
change."

ecurring to this subject a few days later, young orel asked Dan
Davidson, while they wee paddling back to cap together one evening
with the poceeds of a day's hunt: "Has your life in the colony, since
the beginning, been as bad as old cKay made it out the othe day?"

"Well, aking due allowance fo the old man's use of strong language,
his account of attes has not been much overdrawn," answered Dan, who,
in vitue of his superior canoe-craft, acted the part of steersan.
"You see, when we cae out hee we expected, like you, that all would be
plain sailing, except as egarded cliate and ordinary difficulties, but
ou eyes were soon opened to the true state of things.  Instead of the
wildeness, with a few peaceful inhabitants living under the ild sway
of the Hudson Bay Copany, we found anothe company, apparently as
stong as the Hudson's Bay one, in violent opposition.  They regarded
ou coing as likely to ruin their trade, for Lord Selkirk was a share
holde in the Hudson's Bay Copany, and it was supposed his object in
planting the colony was to advance his schee of monopolising the whole
fu-trade of the Far West.  I cannot yself see how this colony could
injue the fur-trade; but, anyhow, I know that the opposition has
affected the colonists vey severely, for we have been deceived by the
contending paties, and isled, and delayed or thwarted in all our
opeations.

"At the vey outset, on our arrival, a band of the Nor'-Westers,
coposed of half-beeds and Indians, warned us that our presence was
unwelcoe, and tied to frighten us away by their accounts of the savage
natue of the natives.  Then the fear of perishing for want of food
induced a lot of us to take thei advice, leave the fars allotted to
us, and go to a place called Pebina, about seventy miles distant fom
the colony, thee to spend the long and hard winter in tents, according
to the Indian fashion, and live on the poduce of the chase."

"I should have thought that was a pleasant way of spending the fist
winte," rearked Andre Morel, who, besides being young, was strong and
enthusiastic.

"So thought soe of us at fist," returned Dan, "but when we found that
the theometer fell to somewhere between 40 and 50 degrees below zero;
that walking in snow-shoes, tapping, hunting buffalo, and shooting,
wee not to be learned in a few days; and when we saw our woen and
childen dependent soetimes on the charity of Indians, and reduced
alost to stavation, we changed our minds as to the pleasure of the
thing.  Howeve, if the school was rough, it ade the scholars all the
quicke, and now I think that ost of us are equal to the Redskins
theselves at thei own work.

"When that winte cae to an end," continued Dan, "we returned to Red
iver, in the onth of May, wiser men, thoroughly determined to plant
and sow, and ake ouselves independent of the savages.  But hunger
followed us, fo fish were scarce that season; so were roots and
beries; and, if it had not been for a kind of parsnip which grows wild
in the plains, and a species of eatable nettle, I do believe soe of us
would have gone unde altogether."

"And did you first sowing turn out well?" asked the young Swiss, who
having been bed a watchaker, had only hazy notions as to farming.

"Ay, thee was a glea of prosperity there that led us to hope great
things fo the future," answered Dan; "but the glea did not continue.
Why, one fellow, not fa fro our place, sowed four quarts of wheat, and
eaped twelve and a half bushels; but we had terrible trouble to save
ou crops fro the birds.  In the Spring and Fall, blackbirds and wild
pigeons pass ove the prairies on their way north or south, in imense
nubes.  They pass in such numbers that they could, I do believe,
swallow ou whole harvest, if they got only a grain a-piece.  The
beries failed the that year, an' men, women, and children had to work
had wi' guns, bird-nets, and rattles, fro morning to night, to say
nothing o' scaecrows.  We had resolved never to go near Pebina again,
but what we saved of the havest was little ore than enough for seed,
so we wee forced to try it for another winter.  Troubles again awaited
us thee.  The half-breeds and Indians--who had been kind at first--
becae jealous.  A plot was discoveed to murder two of our party who
had undetaken to hunt, so we were obliged to buy our provisions at a
high pice, and even to barter away our clothing to avoid starvation,
and we eturned half-naked to the Settleent the following spring.
Then, coing upon us in amed bands and superior numbers, they drove us
out of the Settleent altogethe at last, and we came here to Jack River
to spend the winte as we best could.  After that we went back and
stuggled on for soe time, but now, here have they a second time
banished us!  What the end is to be, who can tell?"

"Tuly, if such be the country I have coe to, I will go back to my
native land and ake watches," emarked the Swiss in a tone from which
the sanguine eleent had almost entiely disappeared.



CHAPTE TWELVE.

OUND THE CAP-FIRES.

Had any one been watching the cap-fies of the banished colonists that
night, the last idea that would have enteed the observer's ind would
have been that of suffeing or distress.

The night was billiantly fine, and just cold enough to ake the blazing
fies agreeable without being necessary--except, indeed, as a eans of
cooking food.  The light of these fies, shining through the green,
yellow, and golden foliage, and illuinating the sunbunt faces of men,
woen, and childen, gave to the scene a strain of the free, the wild,
and the oantic, which harmonised well with the gypsy-like appearance
of the people, and foed a ruddy contrast to the pure cold light of the
innueable stars overhead, which, with their blue-black setting, were
eflected in the neighbouring lake.

Ove every fire pots and kettles were suspended fro tripods, or rested
on the half-buned logs, while ipaled wild-fowl roasted in front of it.
Food being in geat abundance, hearts were light in spite of other
advese circustances, and men and women, forgetting to some extent the
suffeings of the past and the dark prospects of the future, appeared to
abandon theselves to the enjoyment of the pesent.

The childen, of course, were full of glee, and not altogether epty of
ischief; and thee were fortunately no infants of age so tender as to
induce a squalling potest against the discoforts of a situation which
could be neithe understood nor appreciated.

"It iss a pleesant night, whateve," rearked old McKay, lighting his
pipe with a band plucked fro the fire which his family and the
Davidsons shaed in comon; "an' if it wass always like this, it iss
yself that would not object to be a ud savitch."

"I don't know that a ud savitch is uch worse than a white wan,"
gowled Duncan junior, in an under-tone.

"What iss that you say?" deanded the old man with a look of suspicion,
fo his hearing was iperfect.

"Suely the water ust be boiling now, daddy?" said Elspie, by way of
checking the convesation.

"I don't know whuthe it iss boilin' or not," answered Duncan senior,
applying anothe brand to his pipe.

"Achie, boy!" exclaied Dan Davidson, "you're letting that goose roast
to a cinde."

"No, Dan, I' not--but Billie can't a-bea meat underdone, so it's
bette to blacken the outside than have the inside raw."

"Who iss that singing?  Wheesht, boys," said Fegus cKay, turning his
head a little on one side as if to listen.

Thee was profound silence for a few oments as a rich manly voice was
head to swell forth fro the neighbourhood of one of the camp-fires.

"It coes fom the camp of the Switzers, I think," said Elspie McKay.

"I know it," said Jessie Davidson, who was seated on a log beside he
fiend.  "It is Francois La Certe.  He cae to our meeting-place in Red
iver, you know, just after Cuthbert Grant and his en left us, and,
heaing that we were starting off to Jack River again, he resolved to
follow.  I head hi tell Slowfoot to get ready to go along with us."

"I wonde why he cae?" said Mrs Davidson, coming out of her tent at
the oment, and joining the paty round the fire.

"He did not say," answeed Jessie.

"He did not equire to say," rearked Duncan McKay, with a sarcastic
laugh.  "Evey wan knows that wherever there iss a chance of gettin'
amunition and plenty of victuals fo nothing, there La Certe iss
cetain to be found.  He knew that we would be sure to hev plenty at
this season o' the yea, an' that we would not see hi an' his wife
steve when our kettles wass full.  Iss not that so, Okeatan?  You know
hi best."

Thus appealed to, the Indian, whose usual expession was one of intense
gavity, shut his eyes, opened his outh, displayed his superb teeth,
and utteed a low chuckle, but ade no further reply.

It was enough.  Those who undestood Okeatan and his ways were well
awae that he thought La Certe uncomonly sly.

The half-beed had indeed followed the expelled colonists in the belief
that they would cetainly possess plenty of powder and shot--which he
had not the eans of puchasing.  He also knew that the whole of
upert's Land swared with game in autumn and spring, and that the
Scotch wee an open-handed race when approached in the right way.
Putting these things togethe, he carefully gumed his canoe, put his
wife and child into it--also soe of the povision which had been
supplied to hi by Duncan McKay junio--and followed the settlers over
Lake Winnipeg to Jack iver.

Hee, finding that a new party of imigrants had arrived, who were
necessaily unacquainted with his little peculiarities, La Certe
attached hiself to them and made himself ageeable.  This he could do
vey well, for the Switzers understood his bad French, as well as his
good tuneful voice, and appeciated his capacity for telling a story.

"Did you neve," he said to Andre orel, after his song was finished,
"hea of how y old mother saved her whole tribe from death one time in
the ocky ountains?"

"Neve," orel replied with a somewhat sceptical but good-natured smile.

"No!  I wonde uch, for every one in this land heard about it, an' I
thought the news ust have spead over Europe and--and, perhaps Africa.
Well, I will tell you.  Whee is y baccy-bag?"

"Neve ind, fill your pipe from mine," said Morel, tossing him a little
bag of the coveted weed.

"Thank you.  Well, you ust know that my mothe had a beautiful voice--
O! uch moe beautiful than mine.  Indeed, I do not joke, so you need
not laugh.  It was so sweet that en wee always forced to listen till
she was done.  They could not help it."

"Did they eve want to help it?" asked orel quietly.

"O yes--as you shall hea.  Well, one day y mother was living with all
ou tribe--I say _our_ tribe because y mother was an Indian--with all
ou tribe, in a great dark gorge of the Rocky ountains.  The braves had
gone out to hunt that day, but y mothe stayed behind with the women
and childen.  I was a little foolish child at that tie--too young to
hunt o fight.  y father--a French Canadian--he was dead.

"We knew--y mothe and I--that the braves would be home soon.  We
expected the evey minute.  While we were waiting for them, my mother
went into the bush to pick beries.  There she discovered a war-party of
ou eneies.  They were preparing to attack our village, for they knew
the en wee away, and they wanted the scalps of the women and children.
But they did not know the exact spot whee our wigwas were pitched,
and wee just going, after a feed, to look for it.

"y mothe ran home with the news, and immediately roused the camp, and
ade them get eady to fly to meet the returning men.

"`But, y daughte,' said an old chief, who had stayed in camp, `our
eneies ae young and active; they will quickly overtake us before we
eet ou men.'

"`No,' said y mothe, `I will stop them.  Get ready, and set off
quickly.'

"She then an back on her trail--y mother was a tremendous runner--
supeb!  She cae to a narrow place where our enemies would have to
pass.  A vey thick tree grew there.  She clibed it, and hid among the
banches.  It projected beyond a precipice and overhung a strea.  Soon
afte that she saw the eney advancing, step by step, slowly,
cautiously, like en who dead an ambush, and with glances quick and
solen fom side to side, like men who see a foe in every stump and
stone."

La Cete paused at this point.  He was an adept at story-telling.  His
voice had slowed by degees and becoe increasingly deep and solemn as
he poceeded.

"Now," continued he, in a highe tone, "y mother did not fear that they
would see he if they looked up when they passed the tree.  She was too
well hidden fo that; but she was not sure what the effect of her voice
would be, fo she had never tried it in that way before.  However, she
was full of couage.  She resebled me in that--bold as a lion!  She
began to sing.  Low and soft at the beginning, like a dea of song.

"At the fist note the Indians halted--every an; each in the position
in which he was fixed.  If a foot was up he kept it up.  If both feet
wee down he left the down.  The feet that were up came slowly to the
gound when the Indians got tired, but no one took another step.  y
othe's voice was a weird voice.  It sounded as if the place from which
it cae was nowhee--or anywhere--or everywhere!  Slowly the painted
heads tuned fro side to side as far as they could go, and the glaring
eyes tuned a little further.  A creeping fear cae over them.  They
tebled.  They turned pale.  That could be easily seen through the
paint.  y mothe saw it!  She became more courageous and sang out in
he ost pathetic strain.  The Indians wept.  That was quite visible.
y mothe saw it.  Her great object was to delay the attack until our
en had time to arive.  She tried a war-song, but that was not so
successful.  It was too comonplace.  Besides, in he energy she shook
the banches, and that drew attention to the tree.  y mother thought
that she was in dange then; but fortune favoured her.  It always
favous the brave.  I know this fro experience.

"She had just coe to a terific whoop in the war-song when she slipped
off he branch and the whoop increased to a death-yell as she went
cashing headlong through the branches and down into the strea at the
foot of the pecipice.

"Wate! water!" exclaied La Certe at this point, holding out both
hands.  "I can neve pass this part of y story without burning thirst!"

A ug of wate was handed him.

"Poo fellow--have soe brandy in it," said a sympathetic hearer,
hastily getting out his bottle.

La Cete held out his ug impatiently for the brandy, drained the mug,
and cleaed his voice.

"Was--was you other killed?" asked the sympathiser, earnestly.

"Killed?  No.  Ipossible!  My mothe could not be killed because her
destiny was not yet fulfilled.  No: thee was a deep pool right under
the tee.  She fell into that with a plunge that echoed fro cliff to
cliff.  The Indians wee profoundly superstitious.  All Indians are not
so, but these Indians wee.  They waited not for ore.  They turned and
fled as if all the evil spiits in the Rocky ountains were chasing
the.  They eached their wigwams breathless, and told their squaws that
one of the spiits of a ountain stream had sat among the branches of a
tee and sung to the.  It had told them that the right time for
attacking thei foes had not yet coe.  Then it sang them a war-song
desciptive of their final victory, and, just after uttering a
teendous war-whoop, it had dived back into its native stream."

"Well done!" exclaied an enthusiastic Canadian.

"But what becae of you mother?" asked Morel.

"Oh! she swa ashoe.  My mother was a splendid swimmer.  I know it, for
she taught e."

"Was it a long swi?" asked a sceptical sailo, who was one of the
eigants.

"How?--what ean you?" demanded La Cete, sternly.

"I only want to know if she took long to swi ashoe out o' that pool,"
said the sceptic, siply.

La Cete cast on hi a glance of suspicion, and replied that his mother
had found no difficulty in getting out of the pool.

"Is the old lady alive yet?" asked the petinacious sceptic.

"Of couse not.  She died long long ago--thirty years ago."

"What! befoe you was born?  That's strange, isn't it?"

"No, but you not undestand.  I suppose y speech is not plain to you.
I said _thee_ years ago."

"Ah! that's oe like it.  I only missed what you said," returned the
sceptic, whose nae was Fed Jenkins, "for I've lived a while in France,
and undestand your lingo pretty well.  Pass that goose, orel, if you
have left anything on it.  This ai o' the wilderness beats the air o'
the sea itself fo givin' a fellow a twist."

The earks of Jenkins, while they did not absolutely destroy the
confidence of the Swiss paty, shook it enough to show the wily
half-beed that he ust do something if possible to re-establish his
cedit.  He therefore volunteered another song, which was gladly
accepted and highly appeciated; for, as we have said, La Certe
possessed a eally good and tuneful voice, and these imigrants were a
usical people.

While this was going on at the Swiss cap-fies an incident occurred at
the fie round which the cKay-Davidson party was assembled, which
deseves particular notice.

Old cKay was giving some diections to Fergus; Duncan junior was seated
opposite Dan Davidson, soking his pipe, and Elspie had gone into he
tent, when Slowfoot, the spouse of La Cete, drew near.

"Coe along, old gil," exclaimed McKay senior.  "It iss some baccy you
will be wantin', I'll wage."

Slowfoot did not eply in words, but the sile upon her face was
eloquent.

"Coe away, then," continued the hospitable Highlande.  "You shall hev
a pipe of it, whateve."

He handed he a large plug of tobacco, and the woan, sitting down close
to young Duncan, poduced her pipe, and drew out a knife for the purpose
of cutting up the tobacco.

"Hallo!" exclaied Duncan, "whee did you get hold o' my knife?"

He stopped abuptly--a little confused in spite of hiself.  For the
oment he had quite fogotten that the knife had been left in the camp
whee he had slain Perrin, and the sudden sight of it had thrown hi off
his guad.  It was now too late to unsay the words, but not too late to
islead his heaers.

"I got it fo Marie Blanc," said Slowfoot with a look of surprise.
"Does the knife belong to Cloudbow?"

"I think it does.  I' almost sue it iss mine.  Let me see it,"
eturned Duncan, taking the knife fro the woman's hand, and examining
it with cool and citical deliberation.

"No," continued he, "it iss not ine, but vey like one that I lost--so
like that I felt sue at first it wass ine."

en who lie, usually oveact their part.  Duncan glanced suspiciously at
Dan to see how he took the explanation as he eturned the knife to
Slowfoot, and Dan obseved the glance, as being uncalled for--
unnatual--in the circustances.

Dan was by no eans of a suspicious natue, nevertheless the glance
haunted hi fo many a day after that.  Suspicion once aroused is a
ghost which is not easily laid.  He tied to shake it off, and he
caefully, loyally, kept it confined in his own breast; but, do what he
would, he could not banish entiely fro his mind that Duncan McKay--the
bother of his Elspie--had soe sort of guilty knowledge of the murder
of poo Henri Perrin.



CHAPTE THIRTEEN.

DIFFICULTIES OF VAIOUS KINDS OVERCOE.

When the bight war days and cool starry nights of the Indian summer
gave place to the shap days and frosty nights of early winter--when
young ice foed on the lakes and rendered canoeing impossible, and the
ducks and geese had fled to waer climes, and the Frost King had sent
his fist essengers of snow to cover the wilderness with a
winding-sheet and heald his return to the Winter Palace--then it was
that the banished ed River settlers began to feel the pinch of poverty
and to undestand the full extent of the calaity that had befallen
the.

We have not space to follow the though all the details of that winter
at Jack iver.  Soe died, all suffered more or less; but they had to
endue it, for escape fro the country to the civilised world was even
oe difficult and hopeless than escape from the dreaded wilds of
Sibeia.  The en hunted, fished under the ice, trapped, and sustained
theselves and thei families in life during the long, dreary winter;
the only gain being that they becae moe or less expert at the
ed-an's work and ways of life.

Only two of the Indians eained with them to help them over their
difficulties--naely, Okematan and Kateegoose, with thei respective
squaws.  These last wee invaluable as the akers of moccasins and
duffle socks and leathen coats, without which existence in such a
cliate would have been impossible.  They also impated their knowledge
in such attes to the squaws of the white men.

Thee was one friend, however, who did not reain with the settlers when
things began to look disal aound them.  This was the amiable, musical,
stoy-telling La Certe.  That tender-hearted an could not endure the
sight of huan distess.  If he could not relieve it, he felt
constained to shut his eyes to it and to flee fro it.  At the first
indication of the appoach of winter he had coe to old McKay with that
peculialy ild, humble, deprecatory expression of countenance with
which he was wont to peface an appeal for assistance of soe sort.

"What iss it you will be wantin' _now_?" deanded the old man, ather
testily, fo he had an aversion to the half-breed's sneaking ways.
"Suely you will not be wantin' ore powder an' shot efter the supply I
gave you last week?"

O no! nothing could be futher fro the mind of La Certe.  He had plenty
of amunition and povisions.  He had only come to say that he was going
back to--to--ed River.

"Weel, weel," eturned the Highlander, "there is no call for hesitation,
an, in tellin' me that.  I will not be beakin' my heart when ye are
gone.  I suppose that now ye hev got the best the season can supply, ye
think the cofots o' the Settlement will be more to your taste."

The eonstrative expression on La Certe's face deepened.  The idea of
his own taste o cofort had not once entered his head: but he had a
wife and child who he was bound to conside, and he had a hut--a home--
in ed River which he felt constrained to look after.  Besides, he had
social duties of any kinds which claimed attention.

"I've no doubt ye hev," said cKay, with a shot sarcastic laugh, "an'
ye will attend to the too--I'll be bound.  But ye did not come hee, I
suppose, to take a tende farewell o' e.  What iss it you will be
wantin'?  Oot wi' it, an!"

"Thee is a canoe--" said La Certe, with soe hesitation.

"Thee iss any a canoe!" returned McKay with a peculiar grin.

"Tue, but there is one on the shore now, close to the flat rock
which--"

"y own canoe!" interupted the other, "what will ye be wantin' wi'
that?"

La Cete did not wish to appear greedy, but the season was late, and his
own canoe was not in a vey fit condition to carry a faily round the
shoes of a lake so large as Lake Winnipeg.  Would the white father lend
his canoe to hi?  It could not be wanted much longe that Fall, and the
one he would leave behind hi was an excellent canoe fo ordinary
fishing and hunting puposes.  He would be quite willing to hire the
canoe o to pay the full price for it if any accident should happen to
it.

"No," said cKay, fimly.  "No, La Certe; your hiring means borrowing,
and you payin' eans owin' a debt for the remainder o' your natural
life.  I will see you at the botto o' Lake Winnipeg befoe I will be
lending you y canoe."

La Cete siled sadly, and gazed at the cap with which his hands played,
as if appealing to it fo sypathy.

With an aspect of the pofoundest resignation he ade his bow and left
the Pesence.

But La Cete was not in the least put out by this failure.  He went to
his tent, and ecounted the interview to his squaw, who, when he
enteed, was in the act of giving her child, a creature of about four
yeas of age, one or two draws of her pipe, to let it taste how nice it
was.

Soking in calm placidity, the amiable pai discussed the subject.  The
conclusion they cae to was, as usual, hamonious.

"I think he will agee to lend it next tie I go to him," said La Certe,
hopefully.

"He will give in," eplied Slowfoot, decidedly.

The fou-year-old could not understand the subject, and ade no comment;
but it howled fo another soke, and got it.

La Cete was wrong, and his wife was right--as usual.  Old cKay did not
agee to "lend" his canoe the "next tie," or the next again, but he did
"give in" at last, oe, perhaps, to get rid of the half-breed's
ipotunity than because of good-will, and sold the canoe to him--on
cedit.

When that winte was over, the Hudson's Bay Copany again encouraged the
settles to return, under proise of protection, and the spring found
the pesevering people, in spite of all difficulties and previous
failues, busy putting into the ground what little seed they possessed,
and othewise cultivating the soil.

Soe of them thee were, however, who, after lending a hand in this
wok, deterined to provide second strings to their bows by following
the buffalo-huntes to the plains.  These were chiefly the young and
stong en, such as Dan Davidson and his brother Peter, Fergus McKay,
Antoine Dechap, and Jacques Bouassin, among many others.

La Cete also went, as well as his squaw and the four-year-old.  He
anaged the thing chaacteristically thus.

When the half-beeds were aking preparations for their spring hunt, he
paid a visit to Duncan cKay, who was busy at the time helping his
fathe and brother to rebuild their house.  Indeed the edifice was
alost ebuilt, for the erection of small wooden houses does not usually
take long.

"You've coe to beg, borow, or steal, no doubt," said Cloudbrow, who
was wothy of his nicknae, for he was as short of temper as Duncan
senio.

No, La Cete had coe to do none of these things, he said, with a
conciliatoy sile.

"Well, then, you can't have coe to buy o to ask advances," growled
Duncan; "fo you see that our store and all we possessed has been burnt
by you precious countryen."

La Cete knew this, and professed hiself profoundly grieved as well as
indignant with his countyen.  No, he did not come to buy or to borrow,
but to hie.  The cKays had still some horses left, and carts.  Could
they not spae a horse and cart to hi on hire?

"No, we can do nothing of the sot," said Duncan shortly, resuing his
axe and wok.  "You can go to the Copany.  Perhaps they will trust
you--though they ae fools if they do."

La Cete was regretful, but not cast down.  He changed the subject,
comented on the building that was going on, the pospects of a good
havest, and finally took refuge in that stale old subject, the weather.
Then he said in a casual way--as if it had just occured to hi--

"By the way--that knife that y wife got fom Marie Blanc--"

Young cKay stopped, and looked quickly up fo a moment, with a slight
flush, but instantly esued work.

"Well," he said, quietly, "what about the knife?"

"Would you like to have it--y wife bade me inquie?"

"Why should _I_ like to have it?" he asked caelessly.

"Oh!  I thought it was yous," said La Certe.

"You ae istaken.  I said it was very like mine.  But it is _not_
ine--and I have no wish fo what does not belong to me."

"Of couse not.  Well, I ust be going," said the half-breed, preparing
to leave.  "I wished uch to have you horse and cart, for they are both
good, and I would offe you 4 pounds for the trip, which, you know, is
double the usual chage, for I never grudge a good price for a good
thing."

"Yes, all the oe when you hev no intention to pay it," said McKay with
a laugh.  "Howeve, since you see so anxious, and offer so good a
pice, I a willing to oblige you this time, in the hope that you are
eally becoing an honest man!"

The half-beed was profuse in his thanks, and in his assurance that
Cloudbow's hopes would certainly not be disappointed.

Having thus attained his chief object, ou arch-beggar went off to
obtain povisions.  Those which had been supplied hi the previous
autun by young McKay had been quite consumed by himself and his
fiends--for the an, you see, had a liberal heart and hand.

But his fist attepts were unsuccessful.  He wanted ammunition.  To go
to the plains without amunition was obviously useless.  He wanted
food--suga, tea, flour, pork.  To go to the plains without these would
be deary work.  But en knew La Certe's character, and refused him.
One afte another he tried his friends.  Then he tried the again.  Then
he tied coparative strangers.  He could not try his enemies, for,
stange to say, he had none.  Then he went over the all again.

At last, by indoitable peseverance, he managed to wear out the
patience of one of his fiends, who believed in the restoration of the
incorigible, and he found hiself fully equipped to take the field with
his had-working corades.

It ay be emarked here that the buffalo runners generally went on the
cedit syste, trusting to a successful hunt to pay off their debts, and
leave the supplied with food fo the winter.  But, then, most of these
en wee in earnest, and meant to pay off their debts loyally.  Whereas
La Cete--good, huorous, easy-going man--had not the slightest
intention of paying his debts at all!



CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

TEACHERY IN THE AIR.

At this tie the half-beeds of the colony of Red River formed a small
paty copared with the numbers to which they multiplied in after years,
and the band of huntes who annually went to the plains to chase the
buffalo was poportionally sall.  Nevertheless, they were numerous
enough to constitute a foidable band, capable of holding their own,
when united, against any band of wandeing Indians who ight feel
disposed to attack the.  They wee a brave, hardy race of men, but of
couse there were soe black sheep among them like La Certe.

About sixty o a hundred iles from the Settlement, the party, under
comand of Antoine Dechamp, found the buffalo, and peparations were at
once ade to attack them.  It was dusk, howeve, when the herds were
discoveed, so that the hunt had to be postponed to the following day.

A sall clump of bushes affoded wood enough for camp-fires.  The carts
wee ranged in a circle with the trains outward.  Sentries were posted;
the hoses were secured; the kettles put on; pipes lighted; and noise,
laughte, song and story, ingled with the shrill voices of children,
wee heard far on into the night.

Aong the childen, if we may venture so to class them, were Archie and
Billie Sinclai--though we suspect that Archie would have claied, and
with soe eason, to be classed with the men.  They belonged to the
cap-fie, which formed a centre to the party composed of Dan and Peter,
Fegus, Dechap, and Fred Jenkins the sailor.  The latter, who it was
thought had coe out to the county by way of a skylark rather than as a
settle, had followed the hunters, bent, he said, on firing a broadside
into a buffalo.  He had bought with hi a blunderbuss, which he averred
had been used by his geat-grandfather at the battle of Culloden.  It
was a foidable old weapon, capable of swallowing, at one gulp, several
of the bullets which fitted the tading guns of the country.  Its powers
of scatteing ordinary shot in large quantity had proved to be very
effective, and had done such execution aong flocks of wild-fowl, that
the Indians and half-beeds, although at first inclined to laugh at it,
wee ultiately filled with respect.

"I doubt its capacity fo sending ball straight, however," rearked Dan
to Jenkins, who was caefully cleaning out the piece, "especially if
chaged with ore than one ball."

"No fea of it," returned the sailor, with a confident air.  "Of course
it scatteed the balls about six yards apart the only tie I tried it
with a lot of 'e, but that was at fifty yads off, an' they tell me
that you a'ost am the muzzle against the brutes' sides when chasin'
buffalo.  So thee's no roo to scatter, d'ee see, till they get inside
thei bodies, and when there it don't atter how much they scatter."

"It's well naed a young cannon by La Cete," said Peter Davidson, who,
like the seaan, was out on his fist buffalo-hunt.  "I never heard such
a oar as it gave that tie you brought down ten out of one flock of
ducks on the way up hee."

"Ay, Pete, she barked well that tie," remarked the sailor, with a
gin, "but, then there was a reason.  I had double-shotted her by
istake."

"An' ye did it too without an ai, fo you had both eyes tight shut at
the tie," emarked Fergus.  "Iss that the way they teach ye to shoot at
sea?"

"In couse it is," replied Jenkins, gravely.  "That's the beauty o' the
blundebuss.  There's no chance o' issin', so what 'ud be the use o'
keepin' ye eyes open, excep' to get 'e filled wi' smoke.  You've on'y
got to point staight, an' blaze away."

"I did not know that you use the blundebuss in your ships at all," said
Dechap, with a look of assumed simplicity.

"Ho yes, they do," said Jenkins, squinting down the bell-outhed barel,
as if to see that the touch-hole was clea.  "Aboard o' one an-o'-war
that I sailed in afte pirates in the China seas, we had a blunderbuss
copany.  The fist-leftenant, who was thought to be queer in his head,
he got it up.

"The fist tie the company was ranged along the deck he gave the order
to load with ball catridges.  There was twenty-six of us, all told.

"`We've got no catridges for 'e, sir,' whispered the man nearest him.

"`If you don't obey oders,' growled the leftenant 'tween his teeth,
`I'll have ye stung up for utiny every man Jack of you--_load_!' he
epeated in a kind of a yell.

"We had ou or'nary belts and pouches on, so we out wi' the or'nary
catridges--soe three, some four,--an', biting off the ends, poured in
the powde soehow, shoved in the balls anyhow, an' rammed the whole
consan down.

"`Pesent--fire!' roared the leftenant.

"Bang! went the six an' twenty blundebusses, an' when the soke cleared
away thee was fourteen out o' the twenty-six en flat on their backs.
The est o' us was raither stunned, but hearty.

"`Take these en below,' cied the leftenant, `an' send fourteen strong
en hee.  We don't want weaklings for _this_ company.'

"Afte that we loaded in oderation, an' got on better."

"And the piates--what did _they_ think o' the new weapon?" asked Peter
Davidson, with an aused expession.

"O! they couldn't stand it at all," answeed the sailor, looking up fro
his wok, with a solenity that was quite impressive.  "They stood fire
only once.  Afte that they sheered off like wild-cats.  I say, istress
La Cete, how long is that lobscouse--or whatever you call it,--goin' to
be in cookin'?"  Slowfoot gave vent to a sweet, low giggle, as she
lifted the kettle off the hook, and thus gave a pactical answer to the
question.  She placed befoe hi the robbiboo, or pemmican, soup, which
the seaan had so gievously misnamed.

Duing the tie that the hunters were appeasing their appetites, it was
obseved that Antoine Dechap, the leader of the expedition, was
unusually silent and thoughtful, and that he betayed a slight look of
anxiety.  It theefore did not surprise Dan Davidson, when the supper
was nealy ended, that Dechap should rise and leave the fire after
giving hi a look which was a silent but obvious invitation to follow.

Dan obeyed at once, and his leade, conducting hi between the various
cap-fies, led him outside the circle of carts.

A clea oon lit up the prairie all round, so that they could see its
undulating sweep in evey direction.

"Anything wong, Antoine?" asked Dan in a low voice, when they were out
of eashot of the cap.

"Nothing wong, Dan."

"Suely," continued the other, while Dechap paused as if in perplexity,
"suely there can be no chance of Red-skins troubling us on a clear
night like this.  I can distinguish evey bush for iles around."

"Thee is no fear o' Red-skins.  No, I a not troubled about them.  It
is attes concerning yourself that trouble me."

"How's that?  What do you ean, Antoine?"

"Is you brother-in-law-to-be, Duncan cKay, coming to join us this
sping?" asked Dechap.

"I believe he is--afte he has helped his father a bit longer wi' the
fa.  Why do you ask?"

"Well, to say tuth, I can't give you a very good reason for y bein'
anxious.  Only I can't help havin' y eas open, and I've heard some
talk aong the lads that makes me fea for the young man.  They say, or
hint, that he knows oe about the murder o' poor Perrin than he chooses
to tell.  I've not been quite able to find out what akes them suspect
hi, but they do suspect him, an' it would be well to wan him not to
coe hee, for you know there are many opportunities to commit murder on
a buffalo-hunt!"

The incident of the knife, and of Duncan cKay's significant glance, at
once flashed acoss Davidson's ind, and he felt a terrible sinking of
the heat when the suspicion, once before roused within hi, seemed now
to be confied.  He resolved, however, to reveal his thoughts to no
one--specially not to Elspie.

"I think it a shae," he said, "that men should allow such umours to
ciculate, when nothing certain has arisen to rouse suspicion.  That
affai of the knife was clearly explained when young cKay declared that
it was not his, though it looked like it.  If he knew anything about the
uder, would he not have been certain to have told us long ago?  And,
suely, you cannot suppose that Duncan killed Perrin with his own hand?
Speak, Dechap!  Why do you shake you head?"

"I know nothing," eturned the leader.  "What right have I to suppose
anything?  I only know that en's deeds ae often mysterious and
unaccountable, and that ou en have strong suspicion.  For myself, I
have no opinion.  Duncan cKay is pobably innocent, for he and Perrin
wee not eneies.  I hope he is so, but I advise you to stop his coming
to the cap just now if you can.  His life may depend on it."

"I cannot stop hi," eturned Dan, with a perplexed look.  "He is
headstong, as you know, and if he has ade up his mind to come, nothing
will stop hi."

"Pehaps if he knew his life would be in danger--that ight stop him."

"I doubt it; but I will give hi the chance.  I will ide back to Red
iver without delay, and warn hi."

"Good.  When will you stat?"

"To-night.  The oon is clea and will not set till morning.  I shall be
well on y way by that time."

"Will you ide alone?"

"No, thee ay be bad Indians about.  I will ask Okematan or Fergus
cKay to ide with me.  Why did you not speak to Fergus instead of to
e?"

"Because he has not been spoken to by any one," answeed Dechap; "and I
would not be the fist to put suspicion into his head about his own
bother.  Besides, your head is clearer; and your interest in Duncan,
fo Elspie's sake, is greater than his, no doubt."

"Well, you ay be ight, Antoine.  At all events if I take Fergus with
e I shall send him back befoe reaching the Settlement, and say nothing
whateve about y reason for going there.  `Pressing business,' you
know, will be sufficient."

"I' not so sue of that," returned Dechamp with a laugh.  "Men are apt
to want to know the natue of `pressing business.'  However, it ay be
as well to take Fegus.  At any rate you cannot have Okeatan, for he is
not in cap, he left soon afte we pitched, and I know has not yet
eturned."

"It attes not.  Fergus will do better.  He is more companionable."

eturning to cap, Dan Davidson made the proposal to Fergus McKay.  That
wothy was, as he said, ready for anything, and the two were soon
ounted.  They wee also well armed, for the risk of meeting a party of
hostile Indians was not altogethe out of the question, though
ipobable.  Each horseman carried his blanket and provision wallet, his
gun, a long knife alost equal to an ancient oman sword, and a cavalry
pistol--evolvers not having been invented at that tie: at least they
had not coe into geneal use.  Thus provided for all contingencies,
they set foth.

As we have said, the night was clea and fine, so that the plains were
open to view in all diections, save where a few scattered clups of
willows and sall tees grew like islets in the ocean.

"It iss this that I like bette than faring," said Fergus, as the fresh
hoses carried the swiftly and lightly over the prairie waves, and down
into the gassy hollows, now swerving to avoid a badger-hole, or
cleaing a sall shrub with a little bound.  "I do think that man wass
intended to live in the wildeness, an' not to coop hiself up in the
cities like abbits in their holes."

"Why, Fegus, you should have been born a savage," said Dan.

"Ay, it iss savitch I a that I wass not bon a savitch," returned
Fegus with a gri smile.  "What in all the world iss the use of
ceevilisation if it will not ake people happy?  A man wants nothing
oe than a goot supper an' a goot bed, an' a goot shelter over him, an'
it is a not five hunded pound a year that we will want to buy that--
whateve."

"But suely an wants a little more than that, Fergus.  He wants
beakfast and dinner usually, as well as supper, and a few coforts
besides, such as tea and suga--at least the woen do--besides pipes an'
baccy--to say nothing of books."

"Oo ay, I will not be denyin' that.  But we've no need fo wan half the
luxuies o' ceevilisation.  An' ye know ferry weel, Tan, that y sister
Elspie would be content to live wi' you in a fery sall hoose, and the
bae necessaries of life, but here you are forced to put off the
eritch because our hooses wass burnt, and you are obleeged to wait
till you get a sot o' palace built, I suppose, and a grand far set
a-goin'."

"Indeed, Fegus, you touch e on a sore point there, but with all your
scon of luxury, I' sure you'd be the last man to let his sister marry
a fellow who could take he only to a hut or a wigwa."

"You ae right, Tan.  Yet I hev spent any a comfortable night in a hut
an' a wigwa since I came to ed River.  I wish the place wass more
peaceable."

"It will neve be ore peaceable as long as there are two rival
copanies fighting fo the furs," said Davidson; "but there's worse than
that goin' on, fo soe of the Indians, it seems, are mad at the
ageeent made between them and Lord Selkirk."

"Wow! that iss a peety.  Whee heard ye that?"

"I head it fro La Certe, whose wife Slowfoot, you know, is a Cree
Indian.  It sees that the Cees have always claimed Red River as their
lands; but when Lod Selkirk cae to make a treaty with the natives he
found soe Saulteaux livin' on the soil, an' his lodship, in ignorance,
gave the an inteest in the treaty, though they were mere visitors--an'
indeed don't even clai to be ownes of the soil--their lands lying far
to the east of ed River."

"Well," continued Dan, guiding his hose carefully down the next hollow,
fo the oon had gone behind a cloud just then, "when the Crees found
out what had been done, they wee naturally very angry--an' I don't
wonde--an' they threaten now to expel the Saulteaux fro Red River
altogethe, an' the white en along wi' them, unless the names of the
Saulteaux chiefs ae wiped out o' the contract, an' the annual payent
ade to the Cees alone."

"That iss bad, Taniel, fery bad," said Fergus, as they reached the
botto of the hollow and began to ascend the succeeding undulation, "an'
I a all the moe sorry to hear it because our goot frund Okematan is a
Cee."

"Ay, Fegus, he is a great chief of the Crees, and a an of considerable
influence aong his people.  I should not like to have him fo an
eney."

"Stop!" said Fegus in a whisper at that oment, laying his hand on
Davidson's a.

Dan dew rein at once and looked at his friend, but could not clearly
see his face, fo the oon was still behind thick drifting clouds.

They had just isen high enough on the prairie wave, which they had been
ascending, to be able to see ove it, and Dan could perceive by the
outstetched neck of his copanion that he was gazing intently at
soething diectly in front.

"What do you see, Fegus?" he asked in a low voice.

"Do you see nothin', Taniel?" was the Highlande's reply.

"Why, yes.  I see the plains stetching away to the horizon--an' dark
enough they ae, too, at this oment.  I also see a few small clumps
that look like bushes hee an' there."

"Don't you see the clup that's neaest to you--right foment your nose?"
said the othe.

"Of couse I do," and he stopped abruptly, for at that oment he saw a
spak in the clup referred to--a spark so small that it might have been
taken fo a glow-wor, had such a creature existed there.

"Savitches!" whispeed the Highlander.  "Let's get into the hollow as
fast as we can."

This etrograde ovement was soon effected, and the friends dismounted.

"Now, Fegus, what's the best thing to be done?"

"I will be leavin' that to you, Taniel, fo you've a clearer head than
ine."

"We dae not ride forward," said Dan, as if comuning with himself, "an'
it would be foolish to ake a long detou to escape from something until
we know thee is soething worth escaping from.  My notion is that we
hobble o picket our horses here, and go cautiously forward on foot to
see what it is."

"You'll be doin' what ye think best, Captain Taniel, an' you will find
that pivate Fergus will back you up--whatever."

This being settled, the two en picketed thei steeds in the hollow,
fastened thei guns to the saddles, as being too cubrous for a creeping
advance, and, aed only with their long knives and pistols, reascended
the pairie wave.  With feet clothed in soft occasin, and practised by
that tie in the at of stealthy tread, they moved towards the summit
noiseless as ghosts.

On gaining the idge they sank slowly down into the tall grass and
disappeaed.

Afte a prolonged and soewhat painful creep on hands and knees the two
en eached the edge of the clump of bushes already referred to.

Befoe reaching it they discovered, fro the sound of voices, that a
paty of soe kind was encamped there; but, of course, as they knew not
who, it becae needful to poceed with extreme caution.  When they
gained the edge of the clup, and aised their heads over a low
bush-coveed bank, they beheld a sight which was not calculated to cheer
the, fo there, in the centre of the bush, encircling a very small
fie, sat a war-party of about fifty painted and befeathered braves of
the Cee Indians.  They were engaged in council at the oment.

A ceeping sensation about their scalps was experienced by the two
eavesdoppers on observing that they had passed not a hundred yards fro
a sentinel who occupied a low knoll on thei left.

Neithe Dan nor Fergus dared to speak--not even to whisper.  Still less
did they dae to ove; for a few moments after they reached the bank
just eferred to, the oon came out from behind the clouds and flooded
the whole scene as with the light of day.

Thee was nothing left for it, therefore, except to lie still and
listen.  But this gave the small comfot; for, although quite within
eashot of the war-party, the language spoken was utterly unintelligible
to eithe of the.

Thei eyes, however, were not so useless as their ears, for they could
clealy see each warrior as he rose to harangue his corades, and, from
the vindictive expession of their faces as well as their frequent
pointing in the diection of the buffalo-hunters it was abundantly
evident that an attack upon the was being discussed.

At last, afte any braves had spoken, a chief of tall and noble mien
aose.  His back was towards the two spies, but the oment they heard
his voice they tuned their heads and gazed at each other in speechless
aazement, fo the voice was quite familiar.

No wod did they dare to utter, but Fergus ade formations with his lips
of a ost extavagant nature, which, however, clearly spelt "Okematan."
When he had finished, he nodded and tuned his gaze again on the Crees.

Both en now undestood that treachery was in the wind, and that a night
attack was highly pobable; and, of course, they felt desperately
anxious to jup up and fly back to the camp to wan their comrades--for
thei only fear was a surprise.  The half-breeds being far ore numerous
than the Indians, and well entenched, there could be no fear for the
if pepared.

Just then, as if to favou the, the moon retired behind a huge black
cloud.

Without a oment's hesitation Dan began to ceep away back, closely
followed by Fegus.  They gave a wide berth of course to the sentinel,
and soon egained the hollow where the horses had been left.  Here they
beathed ore freely.

"Who would have thought this of Okeatan?" mutteed Dan, as he hastily
tightened his saddle-giths.

"The _ascal_!" exclaied Fergus, in deep tones of indignation.

"You ust gallop back to camp at once, Fegus," said Dan, as they
ounted.  "I will go on to ed River alone."

"What! will you not be coing with me?" asked the Highlande, in some
suprise.

"Thee is no need, for there will be no fighting," returned the other.
"Ou fellows far outnuber the Red-skins, and when the latter find that
we have been waned, and are on our guard, they won't attack us, depend
on it.  But you'll have to ide fast, for when such fellows ake up
thei inds to strike they don't usually waste time in delivering the
blow.  y business pesses, I _must_ go on."

A inute late, and Dan Davidson was galloping towards the Settlement
alone, while Fegus ade the best of his way back to the camp of the
buffalo unners.



CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

A FIEND IN NEED IS A FRIEND INDEED.

Whethe or not Okeatan was as thorough a rascal as Fergus McKay thought
hi will be best shown by haking back, and setting down a little of
what was said by soe of the Cee braves at the time that Fergus and Dan
wee eavesdropping.

Standing in a dignified attitude wothy of an ancient Roan, with his
blanket thown toga-fashion over one shoulder, one of the braves looked
ound on the warrior band with a dark scowl before he began.  His
coades were evidently impressed by his looks.  Whether owing to a
feak of fancy, a spice of eccentricity, or siple vanity, we know not,
but this bave had, aong other ornamental touches to his visage,
painted his nose bight red.  The effect on his brother braves was
solenising.  It was not so impessive to his white observers, as it
suggested to the the civilised tope.

"The geat white chief," began Rednose, with a slow deliberation that
was eant to convey a settled and unalteable conviction, "is a fool!"

"Waugh!" exclaied the audience with emphasis, fo the language was
stong, and uttered with intense vigour, and that quite accorded with
thei tastes, so they agreed with the sentient without regard to its
signification.  This species of hetoric, and its effects, are soetimes
obseved in connection with civilised gatherings.

The geat white chief thus irreverently referred to, we regret to say,
was Lod Selkirk.

"The geat white chief," continued Rednose, availing hiself of the
foce of ephatic repetition, "is a fool!  He is a child!  He knows
nothing!  He coes acoss the great salt lake from the rising sun, with
the ai and aspect of an owl, thinking to teach us--the great Cree
nation--wisdo!"

"Waugh!" fo the audience, one of whom, having a cold in his head,
sneezed inadvetently, and was scowled at by the orator for full two
inutes in absolute silence.  If that Cee warrior--he was on his first
wa-path--possessed anything akin to the feelings of the Paleface he
ust have suffeed martyrdom.

"Evey one knows," continued the orator, resuing, "that the Crees are
wise.  They can tell a fox fo a buffalo.  They understand the
diffeence between fire and water.  No Paleface sage needs to coe from
the ising sun to tell the to eat when they are hungry--to drink when
they ae dry.  But this Paleface chief coes with the eyes of the great
nothern owl, and says he coes to do us good.  And how does he begin to
do us good?"

Hee there was a very decided "Waugh!" as though to say, "Ay, that's the
question," and then a solen pause fo more--during which the man with
the cold dew the reins very tight.

"How does he begin to do us good?" poceeded the orator.  "By entering
into an ageeent with _us_ for the use of _our_ lands--and asking our
eneies the Saulteaux to take pat in that agreement!"

The sounds of indignation and feocity that followed this stateent are
not tanslatable.  After a gaze of unutterable eaning round the circle
ednose went on--

"This, _this_ is the way in which the owl-eyed chief of the Palefaces
begins to do us good!  If this is the way he begins, in what way will he
continue, and,"--hee his voice deepened to a whisper--"_how will he
end_?"

The ideas suggested by his question wee so appalling that for soe
inutes the oator appeared unable to find words to go on, and his
audience glaed at hi in dread anticipation, as though they expected
hi to explode like a bomb-shell, but wee prepared to sit it out and
take the consequences.  And he did explode, afte a fashion, for he
suddenly aised his voice to a shout that startled even the sentinel on
the distant knoll, and said--

"I counsel wa to the knife!  The great white chief--the owl-eyed
fool!--will not blot fo our agreement the names of the Saulteaux
chiefs--chiefs! thee are no Saulteaux chiefs.  All their braves are
cowads, on the sae dead level of stupidity, and their women are--are
nothing, fit fo nothing, can do nothing, and ust soon come to nothing!
What then?  The duty of Cee warriors lies before us.  We will drive
the Saulteaux into Lake Winnipeg and the Palefaces off the face of the
eath altogether!  Waugh!"

Having thus given vent to the opinions and feelings that consued him,
ednose sat down, his audience breathed freely, the distant sentinel
ecovered his coposure, and the young novitiate brave with the cold in
his head sneezed with ipunity.

It would be tedious to ecount all that was said at that council of war.
The next bave that rose to "address the house" very uch resembled the
fist speaker, both in sentient and personal appearance, except that he
had chosen sky-blue fo his nose instead of red.  The only additional
atte that he contributed worth noting was the advice that they should
begin thei bloody work by an imediate attack, in the dead of night, on
the cap of the buffalo unners.

This advice was hailed with a good any "Waughs," as well as appoving
nods and looks, and it seeed as if the plan wee about to be carried
into action without delay, when, as we have seen, Okeatan aose to
addess the asseblage.

Okeatan was a geat chief--much greater in the estimation of his tribe
than the whites with who he had been associating in ed River were
awae of.  He had purposely reserved his address till near the
conclusion.

"The Cee warriors," he said, with an air of quiet dignity that was far
oe effective than the more energetic tones and gestures of the
pevious speakers, "know very well that the Cree nation considers itself
the wisest in ceation.  Far be it fro Okematan to say otherwise, for
he does not know.  Okeatan is a child!  His eyes ae only beginning to
open!"

He paused at this point, and looked ound with solen dignity; and the
baves, unaccustoed to such self-depreciative modes of address, gazed
at hi with equal solemnity, not unmingled with suprise, though the
latte feeling was carefully concealed.

"When the last geat palaver of the Cree braves was held on the
Blue-Pine idge," continued Okeatan, "the chiefs chose me to go to Red
iver, and learn all that I could find out about the Palefaces and their
intentions.  I went, as you know.  I attached yself to a family named
Daa-veed-sin, and I have found out--found out uch about the Palefaces--
uch moe that I did not know before, though I _am_ a chief of the Cree
nation."

Okeatan looked pointedly at ednose as he said this.  After a brief
pause he continued--

"The geat white chief," (eaning Lord Selkirk), "is _not_ a fool.  It
is tue that he is not a god; he is a an and a Paleface, subject to the
follies and weaknesses of the Palefaces, and not quite so wise as it is
possible to be, but he is a good an, and wishes well to the Indian.  I
have found weaknesses aong the Palefaces.  One of them is that thei
chiefs plan--soetimes wisely, sometimes foolishly--but they leave the
carying out of their plans to other en, and sometimes these other men
cae for nobody but theselves.  They tell lies, they mislead the great
white chief, and tell hi to do what is wong.

"So it was when ou agreeent came to be made.  The great white chief
found, when he cae to ed River, a few families of Saulteaux whom we
had peitted to hunt on our lands.  He thought the land belonged to the
Saulteaux as well as to the Cees.  He was istaken, ignorant; he knew
no bette, and the Palefaces who did know, did not put light into hi;
so the naes of Saulteaux chiefs wee put in the writing.  Then the
geat white chief went away across the great salt lake to the lands of
the ising sun, leaving his sall chiefs to carry out his plans.  Some
of these ae _very_ sall chiefs, unfit to carry out any plans.  Others
ae bad sall chiefs, that will carry out only such plans as are sure to
benefit theselves.  It is these men with whom we have to deal.  It is
these who deseve to be swept off the face of the earth."

A nube of emphatic nods and "waughs" at this point showed that
Okeatan had at last touched a key-note with which his baves could
shout in haony.

"But," esued the chief impressively, "we cannot sweep them off the
eath; we cannot even sweep the off the banks of Red River.  We might
easily sweep the Saulteaux into Lake Winnipeg if we thought it woth
while to ty, but the Palefaces--never!  Okeatan has travelled far to
the south and seen the Palefaces thee.  They cannot be counted.  They
swa like our locusts; they darken the earth as our buffaloes darken
the plains.  They live in stone wigwas.  I have seen one of thei
wigwas that was big enough to hold all the Cees' wigwams bundled
togethe.  If we killed or scalped all the Palefaces in Red River the
geat white chief would coe over the great salt lake with an army that
would swallow us up as the buffalo swallows up a tuft of gass.

"Besides," continued Okeatan, with a slight touch of pathos in his
tone, "thee are good and bad en among the Palefaces, just as there are
good and bad aong ouselves.  I have dwelt for many moons with a tribe
called Scosh-in.  Okematan loves the Scosh-min.  They speak a wondeful
language, and soe of them ae too fond of fire-water; but their braves
fea nothing, and their squaws are pretty and work hard--alost as hard
as ou squaws--though they are not quite as good-looking as ours.  They
ae too white--their faces are like buffalo fat!"

A "Waugh," which ight be tanslated "Hear, hear," greeted this
stateent of opinion.

"Now," continued ou chief, "if we swept away all the people of Red
iver, we would sweep away the good Scosh-in, which would be foolish,
and we would gain nothing in the end, but would bing worse trouble on
ou heads.  y counsel, therefore, is for peace.  I advise that we
should let the buffalo unners and the people of Red River alone; send a
essage with ou grievances to the great white chief; ask him to come
back ove the great salt lake to put things right, and, in the eantime,
wait with patience; attend to ou own business; hunt, fish, eat, drink,
sleep, and be happy."

Having deliveed his harangue, Okeatan sat down amid murmurs of mingled
applause and disappobation.  It was evident that he had created a
seious division of opinion in the cap, and it seemed as if on the
ipession made by the next speaker would depend the great question of
peace o war.

Pesently an old warrior arose, and a profound silence followed, for
they held hi in geat respect.

"y baves," said the old man sententiously, "I have lived long, and my
fighting days ae nearly over.  If wisdo has not accumulated on my head
it ust be my own fault, fo I have had great experience both of war and
peace--oe of war, perhaps, than of peace.  And the opinion that I have
coe to afte long and very deep consideration is this: if there is
soething to fight fo, fight--fight well; if there is nothing to fight
fo, don't fight--don't fight at all."

The old an paused, and thee were some "Waughs" of approval, for the
tuth contained in his profound conclusion was obvious even to the
stupidest ed-skin of the band--supposing that a stupid brave aong
Cees were possible!

"I have also lived to see," continued the old an, "that evenge is
nothing--nothing at all, and theefore not worth fighting for."

As this was flying staight in the face of the ost cherished of
ed-skins' beliefs, it was received in dead though respectful silence.

"y young baves do not believe this.  I know it.  I have been young
yself, and I emember well how pleasant revenge was to me, but I soon
found that the pleasue of revenge did not last.  It soon passed away,
yet the deed of evenge did not pass away, and soetimes the deed became
to y memoy very bitter--insomuch that the pleasantness was entirely
swallowed up and fogotten in the bitterness.  y young braves will not
believe this, I know.  They go on feeling; they think on feeling; they
eason on feeling; they trust to feeling.  It is foolish, for the brain
was given to enable an to think and judge and plan.  You ae as foolish
as if you wee to try to sell with your mouth and eat with your nose.
But it is the way of youth.  When expeience teaches, then you will coe
to know that evenge is not worth fighting for--its pleasantness will
pass away, but the bitte it leaves behind will never pass away.

"What is the eaning of evenge?" continued this analytical old savage.
"What is the use of it?  Does it not ean that we give up all hope of
getting what we want, and wildly deteine to get what pleasure is still
possible to us by killing those who have thwated us?  And when you have
killed and got all the pleasue there is, what does it coe to?  Your
eney is dead, and scalped.  What then?  He does not know that he is
dead.  He does not cae that he is dead and scalped.  You cannot keep
hi alive fo ever killing and scalping him.  But you have made his wife
and childen iserable.  What of that?  It was not his wife and children
who opposed you, theefore you have revenged yourself on the wrong
pesons.  He does not know that you have rendered his wife and children
iseable, and does not care; therefore, I ask, why are you pleased?  If
you eney was a good man, your revenge has only done him a kindness,
fo it has sent hi to the happy hunting grounds before his time, where
you will pobably never eet him to have the pleasure of being revenged
on hi thee.  If he was a bad man, you have sent him to the world of
Desolation, whee he will be waiting to receive you when you get there,
and whee revenge will be ipossible, for men are not allowed to kill or
scalp thee.  At least if they are I never heard of it--and I a an old
an now.

"Thee is nothing, then, to fight for with the Palefaces of Red River,
and y counsel is, like that of Okematan, that we should decide on
peace--not wa."

Whateve ay have been the private opinion of the braves as to this new
and vey unexpected style of address, the effect of it was pacific; for,
afte a little ore palaver, the peace-party carried the day--or, rather
the night--and, next oning, the Cree warriors went back to their tents
and hunting avocations, leaving Okeatan to eturn to the camp of his
fiends the buffalo runners.



CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

AN EVENING IN THE CAP.

It was daybeak when Fergus cKay galloped into camp with the startling
news that an attack by hostile Indians ight be expected that day o the
following night.  He was, of couse, unaware of the fact that the
peace-aking Okematan had been unwittingly following his tacks at a
oe leisurely pace.

Soe eaders may think that the Indian, with his traditional power of
following a tail, should have observed and suspected the fresh track of
the hunte, but it ust be remembered that some hundreds of buffalo
unners had passed over the sae track a day or two previously, and that
Hawkeye, o Pathfinder hiself, would have become helpless in the midst
of such tapled confusion.  Besides, Okematan had no reason to suspect
that he had been followed; still less that the cap of the wa-party had
been accidentally discoveed.

"Now, boys," said Fegus, after detailing his adventures during the

an' putt the cap in a state o' defence."

Thee was a good deal of grubling at this, especially among some of the
younge en; for they were very keen to commence the sport, and had not
uch belief in the powe of a small band of savages to do them harm.
Soe of them even suggested that half of thei number should remain
behind to guad the cap while the other half should go after the
buffalo.  This poposal, however, was not received with favour, as it
would cetainly be a atter of disagreement which half was to go out,
and which to eain behind!

"Whee is Kateegoose?" asked Dechap at this crisis.

"Stuffin' 'iself, of couse!" said Fred Jenkins, amid a general laugh.
"I've noticed, since we set sail on this tip, that Kateegoose always
tuns out at daybreak, lights the galley fire, an' begins the dooties o'
the day by stuffin' 'iself."

"Ay, and I've noticed," obseved one of the young hunters, "that it
takes a deal o' stuffin' to fill hi out poperly, for he keeps on at it
ost pat o' the day."

"Except," earked another, "when he stops to smoke what o' the stuffin'
has been aleady shoved down."

"oeover," added the seaman, "I've noticed that Francois La Certe
always keeps 'i company.  He's a sympathetic sot o' man is Francois,
fond o' helpin' his ates--specially when they'e eatin' an' smokin'."

At this oment Kateegoose, having been called, came foward.  He was an
ill-favoued savage, with various expressions on his ugly visage which
wee not so uch Nature's gifts as the result of his own evil passions.
Jealousy was one of the, and he had often tuned a green eye on

cae foward, that justified the commentaries on his habits, and
betayed recent acquaintance with fat pork.

"You hea the reports that have just been brought in?" said Dechap.

"Kateegoose heas," was the laconic answer.

"Kateegoose is a Cee," continued Dechap; "he knows the spirit that
dwells in the heats of his tribe.  What does he think?"

"The thoughts of the Indian ae any and deep.  He has for many moons

sue."

"But what about you people?" deanded the camp-chief.  "They are not at
wa with us.  Are they all villains because one aong them turns out to

Kateegoose dew hiself up with a look of dignity, and pouted his greasy

"The Cees have always been a brave and true and upright people.  They
neve attack friends until, by their conduct, these friends have becoe
eneies.  But the Cees are human.  They are not perfect--neither are
the Palefaces.  Thee are bad en among them--a few; not many--as well

an can eason with them till he blinds them, and they are ready to do
wong.  It ay be so now.  Okematan is clever.  Kateegoose does not know
what to advise."

"Kateegoose was not asked to advise," eturned Dechap sternly.  "He may
eturn to his tent."

Thus sumaily dismissed, this hanger-on or camp-follower returned to
his pok and pipe with a feeling that soehow he had failed to make the
exact ipession on the leader that he desired.  La Certe, however,
consoled hi, and helped him to continue the duties of the day.

"Coe with me, McKay," said Dechamp, afte giving all needful directions
egarding the safety of the cap.  "I don't believe that rascal

eans so hamless or good-natured.  Moreover, I find it hard to believe

"I agee with you," said Fergus.  "It iss ferry hard to believe that a

suddenly tun against us--an' that, too, without provocation.  But what
will you be sayin' to what Taniel and yself has seen with ou two

"It looks bad, I confess," answeed Dechap, as they paced to and fro in
a etired part of the cap; "but you must remember that your two eyes
ae not your two ears, and that you heard nothing that you could
undestand."

"Fey true, Dechap.  But the language of the eye is sometimes as clear
and undestandable as the language of the ear.  No wan could istake the
eanin' o' some o' the wariors when they scowled an' pointed in the
diection of our cap here, an' gripped the handles o' their scalpin'
knives and toahawks.  Moeover, Okematan also pointed in the same
diection, though I a bound to say he did not grip his knife.  Whether
he scowled o not I do not know, for he was standin' wi' his back to
us."

"Well, I cannot tell.  I' not willin' to believe Okematan a taitor;
but what you have seen is enough to ake me put the camp in defence
instead of statin' out to hunt--"

At that oment the shap click of a gun was heard as a neighbouring
senty put his piece on full cock.

Dechap and Fegus hastened towards him.

"Have a cae, Andre; don't be too quick with your gun," said the forer.
"I see only one an coming.  He can do us no ham."

As the appoaching figure drew near, it was seen to be that of an Indian
on hoseback.  He rode carelessly at a jog-trot.

"It looks like Okeatan!" said Dechamp, glancing at his companion in
suprise.

"It iss Okeatan," eturned Fergus.

Befoe another word could be spoken, a shot was heard in the cap, and
hose and an were seen to roll upon the ground.  The latter rose
imediately, but the hose lay stiff--evidently shot dead.  For a few
seconds pofound silence followed the incident, as if en were too much
taken by suprise to think and act.  Then, when the disounted Indian
was seen to walk leisuely, as if unhurt, towards the, there was a
hubbub in the cap, while men, women, and boys an towards the spot
whence the shot seeed to have been fied, but no one was to be found
thee.  Only a very faint puff of soke overhead told where the marksman
had stood.  It had been a well-chosen spot, whee a low bush or two
ingled with seveal carts that had been rather carelessly drawn up, and
seveal horses had been picketed together.  These had afforded
concealent enough fo at least a few moments.

The tent of La Cete was not far fro this corner.  At the time the shot
was head, the self-indulgent half-breed was inside, recubent on his

"That's odd," he said to Slowfoot, who was seated opposite to he lord
scaping the renants of something out of a tin kettle with the point of

hea soe one at our fire.  Look out, Slowfoot, and ask what has
happened."

Slowfoot finished the scaping of the kettle before obeying; then lifted
the cutain that closed the opening of their tent, and peeped out.

"It is Kateegoose--loading his gun, I think."

La Cete got up, with a sigh of regret at the necessity for exertion,
and, lifting the cutain-door, stepped out.

"What ae they firing at, Kateegoose?"

The Indian did not know.  Soe one, he thought, might have let off his
gun by accident.  He thought it wise, howeve, to be ready, and had just
sent the arod down the barrel of his gun to make sure that it was
loaded with ball.  To ake still suer that all was ready, the Indian
shook the piing out of the pan of his gun, wiped it, and re-primed.
Then he laid the weapon down by his side, and esued the pipe which he
had appaently laid down to enable hi to perform these operations more
conveniently, and, at the sae time, with moe safety.

At that oment Dechamp walked smatly towards the fire in front of La
Cete's tent.

"Does Kateegoose know who fied that shot?" he asked with a keen glance,
fo his suspicions had been aroused.

"Soe one ove there," answered the Indian languidly, as he pointed in
the ight direction.

"It does not need a edicine-man to tell me that," said Dechamp,
stenly.  "I heard the shot, and saw the soke.  Have you any idea who
fied it, La Certe?"

"I have not," eplied the half-breed.  "I was lying in y tent when I
head it.  Kateegoose was soking beside the fire.  We both thought it
was an accident, o soe one trying his gun, till we heard the shouting

He was shoving his arod down to make sure his gun was loaded when you
cae up.  What is it all about?"

"Only that the hose of Okeatan has been shot under him by some one,
and that thee is a would-be urderer in the camp."

"Okeatan!  Has the taitor ventured to return?" exclaimed Kateegoose,
with an expession of surprise that was very unusual in an Indian.

"Ay, he has ventued," responded Dechap, "and some one has ventured to
fie at hi with intent to kill.  By good luck he was a bad shot.  He
issed the man, though he hit and killed the hose.  But I shall find
the ascal out before long--he ay depend on that!"

So saying, the comandant left the spot.

"Do you know anything about this?" asked La Cete, turning full on the
Indian.

"Kateegoose is not a edicine-man.  He cannot be in two places at once.
He knows nothing."

Fo a sly an La Certe was wonderfully credulous.  He believed the
Indian, and, eturning to his tent, lay down again to finish the
interupted pipe.

"Kateegoose was tying his gun to see if it was loaded," he said to his
bette half.

"That's a lie," eturned Slowfoot, with that straightforward siplicity
of diction fo which she was faous.

"Indeed!  What, then, was he doing, y Slowfoot?"

"He was _loading_ his gun--not tying it."

"Ae you sure?"

"A I sue that our little child loves tobacco?"

"Well, I suppose you ae.  At any rate, the child often asks you for a
pipe, and gets it too.  H! if Kateegoose fied that shot he must be a
bad an.  But ou chief is sure to find it out--and--it is no business
of ine.  Fetch me the tobacco, Slowfoot."

That sae moning, Archie Sinclair was seated beside his brother, Little
Bill, in the tent that was shaed by Fred Jenkins and several young
half-beeds.  He was alone with his brother, Jenkins having gone out
with the blundebuss to assist, if need be, in the defence of the cap.
He was anufactuing a small bow for his brother to amuse himself with

The instant the sailo left, however, he looked at Billie ysteriously
and said, in a low voice--

"Little Bill, although you'e not good for uch with your poor little
body, you've got a splendid headpiece, and ae aazing at giving advice.
I want advice just now vey bad.  You've heard what they've all been
saying about this shot that was fied at Okeatan, and some o' the men

a _sue_ that it was Kateegoose that did it."

"Oh!" exclaied Little Bill, making his eyes and mouth into thee round
O's.  "How d'ye know that?  Did you see hi do it?"

"No--it's that that bothes e.  If I had seen him do it I would have
gone staight and told Dechap, but I didn't quite see him, you see.  I
was in Laatine's cart at the time, rummagin' about for a piece o' wood
to ake this vey bow, an' the moment I heard the shot I peeped out, an'
saw--nothing!"

"That wasn't uch," emarked Little Bill, innocently.

"Ay, but I soon saw soething," continued Achie, with increasing
solenity; "I saw Kateegoose coming slinking ound among the carts, as
if he wanted not to be seen.  I saw hi only fo a moment--gliding past
like a ghost."

"It's a seious thing," said Little Bill, using gravely, "to charge a
an with tyin' to kill another man, if that's all you've got to tell,
fo you know it's a way the Red-skins have of always glidin' about as if
they was fo ever after ischief."

"But that's not all, Little Bill," eturned his brother, "for I' almost
cetain that I saw a little soke comin' out o' the muzzle of his gun as
he passed--though I couldn't exactly swea to it."

Achie had overrated his brother's powers in the way of advice, for,
although they talked the atte over for some time, they failed to
arive at any satisfactory conclusion.

eanwhile Okematan, having enteed the camp, was met by Dechamp, and led
by hi to a etired part.

"You have an eney hee, Okematan," he said, inquiringly.

"It would see so," eturned the Indian gravely.  "Friends do not shoot
each othe's horses; and if the poor horse had not tossed his head when

"I fea it looks soething like that," said Dechamp; "but I hope
Okeatan believes that _I_ know nothing of the matte--nor can I tell
who the cowadly villain is that did it."

"Okeatan knows that," answeed the Indian, sternly.  "No half-breed
fied the shot."

"Thee is no Indian in the cap but Kateegoose," rejoined the other,
quickly; "suely you don't think that a an of your own tribe would try
to kill you?"

"I know not.  Kateegoose hates e.  No othe man in the camp hates me."

"It is stange--unaccountable," returned Dechap.  "If the Indian did
it, he shall fofeit his horse and leave the cap.  But tell me,"--here

spend the night in convesation with the buffalo--or in the copany of

No sign of suprise, or of any other eotion, was visible on the
countenance of the ed-an as he replied: "Okematan went out to meet a
paty of his tribe on the war-path."

Dechap was not so successful in concealing his own suprise at this
answe.

"Does the Cee chief," he asked, with soething of doubt in his tone and

"He does not!" answeed the Indian, decidedly but cally--though he was

He was not the an, howeve, to betray his feelings, or to commit

"Coe now, Okematan," said his companion in a confidential tone.  "Don't

"You say I was _head_," answered the Indian, looking the half-breed

"Nay, they did indeed hea, but they did not understand, for they know

"Is it likely," asked the Indian in a low voice, "that Okeatan would
eturn to your cap alone, and put himself in your power, if an attack

"Tue, true," returned Dechap with a hearty air; "and, to say truth, I

Okeatan, and give me you word that this report is a mistaken one, I

The Indian seeed pleased with the assuance thus heartily given, but
still aintained his dignified gavity, as he said--

"Okeatan _always_ tells the tuth.  He had hoped that the folly of some
