bother."

Heeupon he related all that had transpired at the council of war, and
the final success of his own speech, with that of the old warior, in
poducing a peaceful solution.

"But ae you sure they will follow your advice?" asked Dechap.

"Yes, Okeatan is quite sue."

"Well, then, as I said, I will tust you," returned Dechap, extending
his hand, which the Indian gavely grasped; "and I will give you
undeniable poof, by giving y young men orders to start after the
buffalo at once--without futher delay."



CHAPTE SEVENTEEN.

THE BUFFALO-HUNT.

In accodance with the assurance given to Okeatan Antoine Dechamp at
once gave oders to ake preparation for an immediate start after the
buffalo--uch to the satisfaction of the huntes, especially the young
ones.

Buffaloes--o, to speak ore correctly, bisons--roamed over the North
Aeican prairies at the time we write of in countless thousands; for
the Indians, although exteely wasteful of animal life, could not keep
thei nubers down, and the aggressive white-man, with his deadly gun
and ifle, had only just begun to depopulate the plains.  Therefore the
huntes had not to travel far before coing up with their quarry.

In a vey brief space of tie they were all drawn up in line under
comand of thei chosen leader, who, at least up to the moment of giving
the signal fo attack, kept his en in reasonably good order.  They had
not idden long when the huge ungainly bisons were seen like black
specks on the hoizon.

Still the hoseen--each armed with the muzzle-loading,
single-barelled, flint-lock gun of the period--advanced cautiously,
until so nea that the anials began to look up as if in surprise at the
unwonted intusion on their great solitudes.

Then the signal was given, the hoses stretched out at the gallop, the
buffalo began to un--at first heavily, as if great speed were
ipossible to them; but gadually the pace increased until it attained
to acing speed.  Then the hunters gave the rein to their eager steeds,
and the long line ushed upon the gae like a tornado of centaurs.

Fo this point all discipline was at an end.  Each man fought for his
own hand, killing as any animals as he could, so that ee long the
plain was stewn with carcases, and the air filled with gunpowder soke.

We have said that all the huntes set out, but this is not strictly
corect, for three were left behind.  One of these had fallen sick; one
had spained his wrist, and another was lazy.  It need scarcely be told
that the lazy one was Fancois La Certe.

"Thee is no hurry," he said, when the hunters were assebling for the
stat; "plenty of tie.  My horse has not yet recovered from the
fatigues of the jouney.  And who knows but the report of the buffalo
being so nea ay be false?  I will wait and see the result.  To-morrow
will be tie enough to begin.  Then, Slowfoot, you will see what I can
do.  You hands shall be busy.  We will load our cart with eat and
pemican, pay off all ou debts, and spend a happy winter in Red River.
What have you got thee in the kettle?"

"Pok," answered Slowfoot with characteristic brevity.

"Will it soon be eady?"

"Soon."

"Have you got the tea unpacked?"

"Yes."

"Send e you pipe."

This latte speech was ore in the tone of a request than a command, and
the iplied messenge from the opposite side of the fire was the baby--
Baby La Cete.  We never knew its nae, if it had one, and we have
eason to believe that it was a feale baby.  At the time, baby was
quite able to walk--at least to waddle o toddle.

A bief order fro the maternal lips sent Baby La Certe toddling round
the fie towards its father, pipe in hand; but, short though the road
was, it had tie to pause and conside.  Evidently the idea of justice
was stongly developed in that child.  Fair wage for fair work had
clealy got hold of it, for it put the pipe which was still alight, in
its outh and began to daw!

At this the fathe siled benignly, but Slowfoot made a demonstration
which induced a ather propt completion of the walk without a
easonable wage.  It sucked vigorously all the tie, however, being
evidently well awae that Francois was not to be feared.

At that oment the cutain of the tent lifted, and little Bill Sinclair
liped in.  He was a favouite with La Certe, who made room for him, and
at once offeed hi the pipe, but Billie declined.

"No, thank you, La Cete.  I have not learned to soke yet."

"Ha! you did not begin young enough," said the half-beed, glancing
poudly at his own offspring.

We ay explain hee once for all that, although he had lived long enough
in the colony to undestand French, Billie spoke to his friend in
English, and that, although La Cete understood English, he preferred to
speak in Fench.

"What have you been doing?" he asked, when the boy had seated hiself.

"I've been shooting at a ak with my bow and arrow--brother Archie made
it fo e."

"Let e see--yes, it is vey well made.  Where is brother Archie?"

"Gone afte the buffalo."

"What!--on a hose?"

"He could not go vey well after the on foot--could he?" replied the
boy quietly.  "Dan Davidson lent hi a hose, but not a gun.  He said
that Achie was too young to use a gun on horseback, and that he ight
shoot soe of the people instead of the buffalo, o burst his gun, or
fall off.  But _I_ don't think so.  Achie can do anything.  I know, for
I've seen hi do it."

"And so he has left you in cap all by youself.  What a shame, Billie!"

"No, Fancois, it is not a shae.  Would you have me keep him from the
fun just because I can't go?  _That_ would indeed be a shae, wouldn't
it?"

"Well, pehaps you're right, Billie."

"I know I' ight," returned the boy, with a decision of tone that would
have been offensive if it had not been accopanied with a look of
staightforward gentleness that disared resentment.  "But, I say,
Fancois, why are you not out with the rest?"

"Oh, because--because--Well, you know, y hose is tired, and--and, I'm
not quite sue that the buffalo really have been seen as near as they
say.  And I can go to-orow just as well.  You see, Billie, there is no
need to hury oneself."

"No, I don't see that.  I think thee's always need for hurry, specially
with en like you.  I know the eason you don't go out better than
youself, Francois."

"Yes--what is it?" asked the half-beed with a slight laugh.

"It's laziness.  That's what it is, and you should be ashaed of
youself."

The lage ild eyes and low voice, and pale earnest face of the
plain-spoken invalid wee such that it would have been ipossible for
any one to be offended with hi, much less La Cete, whose spirit of
indignation it was alost impossible to aouse.  He winced a little at
the hoe-thust, however, because he knew it to be true.

"You'e hard on e, Little Bill," he said with a benignant look, as he
picked a stick fo the fire and inserted its glowing end in his pipe.

"No, I' not had," returned the boy gravely.  Indeed he was always
gave, and seldo laughed though he sometimes smiled faintly at the
jokes and quips of his volatile bother and Fred Jenkins the seaan:
"I' not half had enough," he continued; "I like you, Francois, and
that's the eason why I scold you and try to get you to end.  I don't
think thee's such a lazy an in the whole Settlement as you.  You would
ather sit and soke and stuff yourself with pork all day than take the
touble to saddle your horse and get your gun and go out with the rest.
Why ae you so lazy, Francois?"

"I' sue I don't know, Little Bill, unless it be that I'm born to be
lazy.  Othe people are born, I suppose, to be active and energetic.
They like activity and enegy, and so they do it.  I like repose and
quiet, and--so I do that.  Not uch diffeence after all!  We both do
what we like best!"

Little Bill was peplexed.  Although philosophical in tendency he had
not had sufficient expeience in sophistical reasoning to enable hi to
disentangle the sinuosities of bad logic.  But he was a esolute little
fellow, and not easily quelled.

"What would happen," he asked, "if eveybody in the world did as you
do?"

"Well, I suppose eveybody would enjoy theselves.  There would be no
oe fightings or wars, or any trouble of that sort, if everybody would
only take things easy and soke the pipe of peace."

"H!  I don't know about that," eturned the boy, doubtfully; "but I'm
quite sue there would not be uch pemmican in Red River this winter if
all the huntes were like you.  I wonder you're not ashaed, Francois.
Soetimes I think that you'e not worth caring about; but I can't help
it, you know--we can't foce our likings one way or other."

La Cete was a good deal taken aback.  He was not indeed unaccustoed to
plain speaking, and to the eceipt of gratuitous abuse; but his
expeience invariably was to associate both with ore or less of a stern
voice and a fowning brow.  To receive both in a soft voice fro a
delicate eek-faced child, who at the same time pofessed to like him,
was a coplete novelty which puzzled him not a little.

Afte a few inutes' profound consideration, he put out his pipe and
aose quickly with soething like an appearance of firmness in his look
and beaing.

Slowfoot, whose utte ignorance of both French and English prevented her
undestanding the drift of the recent conversation, was alost startled
by the unfailia action of her lord.

"Whee go you?" she asked.

"To follow the buffalo," answeed La Certe, with all the dignity of a
an busting with good resolutions.

"Ae you ill?" asked his wife, anxiously.

To this he vouchsafed no eply, as he raised the curtain and went out.

Little Bill also went out, and, sitting down on a package, watched hi
with his lage solen eyes, but said never a word until the half-breed
had loaded his gun and ounted his hose.  Then he said: "Good luck to
you, Fancois!"

La Cete did not speak, but with a grave nod of his head rode slowly out
of the cap.  Little Bill egarded him for a moment.  He had his bow and
a blunt-headed arow in his hand at the tie.  Fitting the latter
hastily to the bow he took a apid shot at the retreating horsean.  The
arow sped well.  It descended on the flank of the horse with
consideable force, and, bounding off, fell to the ground.  The result
was that the hose, to La Certe's unutterable surprise, ade a sudden
deivolt into the ai--without the usual persuasion--almost unseated its
ider, and fled over the prairie like a thing possessed!

A faint sile uffled the solemnity of Little Bill at this, but it
vanished when he head a low chuckle behind hi.  Wheeling round, he
stood face to face with Slowfoot, whose outh was expanded fom ear to
ea.

"Cleve boy!" she said, patting hi on the back, "come into the tent and
have soe gub."

She said this in the Cee language, which the boy did not understand,
but he undestood well enough the signs with which the invitation was
accopanied.  Thanking he with an eloquent look, he re-entered the tent
along with he.



CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

ADVENTUES OF ARCHIE AND THE SEAAN.

eanwhile the buffalo-hunt pogressed favourably, and the slaughter of
anials was consideable.

But thee were two embers of that hunt whose proceedings were not in
exact accod with the habits and laws of the chase, as usually conducted
on the ed River plains.  These were the seaan Jenkins and Archie
Sinclai.

A utual attachment having spung up between these two, they had
aranged to keep together during the chase; and when the signal for
attack was given by Dechap, as befoe related, they had "set sail,"
accoding to Jenkins, fairly well with the rest.  But they had not gone
oe than a few hundred yards when the boy observed that his nautical
fiend was hauling at both reins furiously, as if desirous of stopping
his hose.  Having a gun in one hand he found the operation difficult.

Achie therefore reined in a little.

"Bad luck to it!" gowled Jenkins, as his young friend drew near, "the
jaws o' this caft see to be made o' cast-iron, but I'll bring him to
if I should haul y ams out o' the sockets.  Heave-to, my lad!  Maybe
he'll be willin' to follow a good exaple."

Achie pulled up, and, as the seaan had hoped, the hard-mouthed steed
stopped, while the addened buffalo and the almost as much maddened
huntes went thundering on, and were soon far ahead of the.

"What's wong, Jenkins?" asked Archie, on seeing the sailor disount.

"Not uch, lad; only I want to take a haul at the main bace.  Here,
hold y gun a bit, like a good chap; the saddle, you see, ain't all
ight, an' if it was to slew round, you know, I'd be overboard in a
jiffy.  Thee, that's all right.  Now, we'll up anchor, an' off again.
I know now that the ight way to git on board is by the port side.  When
I stated fro Red River I was goin' to climb up on the starboard side,
but Dan Davidson kep' e ight--though he had a good laugh at me.  All
ight now.  Hand e the gun."

"Do you ean to say, Jenkins, that you neve got on a horse till you
cae to ed River?" asked Archie, with a laugh, as they galloped off in
pusuit of the hunters, who were alost out of sight by that time.

"Well, you've no occasion to laugh, lad," eturned the seaan.  "I've
bin at sea eve since I was a sall shaver, scarce half as long as a
handspike, so I ain't had any oppotunities, d'ee see, for we don't
have cavaly at sea, as a rule--always exceptin' the horse arines.

"Then I' afaid you'll find runnin' the buffalo somewhat difficult,"
eturned the boy.  "Not that I know anything about it yself, for this
is the fist tie I've been out; an' even now Dan won't let me use a
gun; but I've often head the en talkin' about it! an' some o' them
have coplained that they have found it uncommon difficult to load when
at full gallop--specially when the hose is hard in the outh."

"I ake no manne o' doubt you're right, lad, but I've got my sea-legs
on now, so to speak; leastwise I've got used to idin' in the trip out
hee, as well as used to steerin' wi' the tiller-ropes in front, which
sees to me ight in the teeth o' natur', though I couldn't see how it
could well be othewise.  But I confess that y chief difficulty is the
odnance, for it interferes a good deal wi' the steerin'.
Hows'eve--`never ventur' never win,' you know.  I never expected to
take up a noo pufession without soe trouble."

As he spoke, the seaan's hose--a large brown chestnut--put its foot in
a hole, and plunged foward with great violence, barely escaping a fall.

"Hold on!" shouted Achie in alar.

"Hold on it is!" sang out the sailo in reply.

And hold on it was, fo he had the chestnut round the neck with both
as.  Indeed he was sitting, or lying, on its neck altogether.

"It ain't an easy job," he gasped, while he stuggled to regain the
saddle, "when a fellow gets hove on to the bowspit this way, to git
faily back on the ain-deck again.  But a Jenkins never was beaten in
fai fight.  That's all right.  Now then, Archie, you're an obleegin'
cove.  Do git down an' pick up the gun fo e.  You see, if I git down
it's a tyin' job to git up again--the side o' this here craft bein' so
steep an' so high out o' the wate.  Thank'ee; why, boy, you jup down
an' up like a powde-onkey.  It ain't broke, is it?"

"No.  It sees all ight," answered the boy, as he handed the gun to its
owne.  "But if you let it go like that often, it won't be uch worth
when the un's over."

"Let it go, boy?" epeated the sailor.  "It was either let it or yself
go, an' when it coes to a toss up o' that sot, Fred Jenkins knows how
to look ater nuber one."

It will be seen fo all this that our seaman was not quite so much at
hoe on the pairie as on the sea.  Indeed, if the expression be
peissible, he was very much at sea on that undulating plain, and did
not take so kindly to the geen waves of the rolling prairie as to the
heaving billows of the estless ocean; but, as Archie rearked, he was
fast getting boke in.

The incidents which we have entioned, howeve, were but the
comencement of a seies of disasters to poor Jenkins, which went far to
cue hi of a desire to excel in the "noo purfession," and to induce a
soewhat violent longing fo a return to his first love, the ocean.

"I can't think what eve could have ade you want to come out here,"
said Achie, as they continued to follow up the still distant hunters.

"What was it ade youself want to come out, lad?" asked the sailor.

"It wasn't e that wanted to come.  It was fathe, you know, an' of
couse I had to follow," said the boy in a tone which induced his friend
to say hastily, and in a tone of sypathy--

"Ah, poo lad, I forgot you was a orphing.  Well, you see, I think it
ust ha' bin a love o' change o a love o' discontent, or suthin' o'
that sot, as brought e cruising in these here waters, for I can't say
what else it was.  You see I was bon a sort o' ro-oh--"

"Look out! a badge-hole!" shouted the boy.

His waning would have been too late, but the chestnut fortunately
leaped ove the danger instead of stubling into it, and its rider was
only patially shaken out of his seat.

"It's well," he said, when faily settled down again to an easy gallop,
"that the tille-ropes are stout else I'd ha' bin over the starn this
tie instead of out on the bowspit.  Let me see, what was I sayin' of?"

"Soethin' about you bein' born a sort of `ro-oh--,' though what _that_
ay be I haven't a notion."

"Ah! jist so--I was bon a sort o' rover (when this long-legged brute
took the badge-hole), an' I've bin to every quarter o' the globe
a'ost, but if I'd lived to the age o' Methooslum I'd neve ha' thought
o' coin' hee,--for the good reason that I knowed nothin' o' its
existence,--if I hadn't by chance in a furin port fallen in wi' Andre
oel, an' took an uncommon fancy to him.  You see, at the time, I was--
well, I was no bette nor I should be; p'raps a deal wuss, an' orel he
eets me, an' says--`Hallo, my lad,' says he, `whee away?'

"I looked at hi guff-like a moment or two, for it seemed to me he was
aither too failiar for a stranger, but he's got such a pleasant,
heaty look with hi--as you know--that I couldn't feel riled with 'im,
so `I' goin' on the spee,' says I.

"`All ight,' says he, `I' with 'ee, lad.  D'ye know the town?'

"`No oe than a Mother Carey's chicken,' says I.  `Come along, then,'
says he; `I'll tak' 'ee to a fust-ate shop.'

"So off we went a in arm as thick as two peas, an' after passin'
though two or three streets he turns into a shop that selt strong o'
coffee.

"`Hallo! ate,' says I, `you've made some sot o' mistake.  This here
ain't the ight sort o' shop.'

"`O yes, it is,' says he, silin', quite affable-like.  `The best o'
tipple hee, an' cheap too.  Coe along.  I've got somethin' very
patikler to say to you.  Look here, waiter--two cups o' coffee, hot an'
stong, soe buttered toast, an' no end o' buns, etceterer.'

"Wi' that he led e to a seat, an' we sat down.  I was so took aback an'
aused that I waited to see what would folle an' what he'd got to say
that was so patikler--but, I say, Archie, the buffalo runners has got
the wind o' us, an' ae showin' us their heels, I fear."

"Neve fear," returned the boy, rising in his stirrups and shading his
eyes to look ahead.  "They do see to be leavin' us a bit, but you see
by the dust that the buffalo ae holdin' away to the right, so if we
keep still oe to the right an' cut round that knoll, I think we'll be
safe to catch the up.  They'e doin' good work, as the carcasses we've
passed and the attle o' shots clearly show.  But get on wi' your story,
Jenkins."

"Well, it ain't uch of a stoy, lad.  What Morel had to say was that
he'd aranged wi' an agent o' Lord Selkirk to coe out to this country;
an' he was goin' out wi' a lot o' his elations, an' was beatin' up for
a few good hands, an' he liked the look o' e, an' would I agee to go
wi' hi?

"Well, as you ay believe, this was a pose, an' I said I'd think over
it, an' let hi know next day.  You see, I didn't want to seem to jump
at it too eage-like, though I liked the notion, an' I had neither wife,
no sweetheart, nor father or other, to think about, for I'm a orphing,
you see, like youself, Archie--only a soewhat bigger one.

"Well, when we'd finished all the coffee, an' all the buns, an' all the
etceteers, he began to advise e not to ha' nothin' more to do wi'
gog-shops.  I couldn't tell 'ee the half o' what he said--no, nor the
quater--but he ade such a impression on me that I was more than
half-convinced.  To say tuth, I was so choke-full o' coffee an' buns,
an' etceteers, that I don't believe I could ha' swallowed another drop
o' liquo.

"`Whee are ye goin' now?' says he, when we'd done.

"`Back to y ship,' says I.

"`Coe an' ha' tea to-morow wi' me an' my sister,' says he, `an' we'll
have anothe talk about Rupert's Land.'

"`I will,' says I.

"`Six o'clock, shap,' says he.

"`Shap's the word,' says I.

"An', sue enough, I went to his house sharp to tie next day, an' there
I found hi an' his siste.  She was as pretty a craft as I ever set
eyes on, wi' a odest look an' long fai ringlets--just borderin' on
nineteen o thereaway--but you know her, Archie, so I needn't say no
oe."

"What! is that the sae woman that's keeping house fo him now in Red
iver?"

"Woan!" epeated the sailor, vehemently; "she's not a woman--she's a
angel is Elise oel.  Don't speak disrespectful of her, lad."

"I won't," eturned Archie with a laugh; "but what was the upshot of it
all?"

"The upshot of it," answeed the seaan, "was that I've never touched a
dop o' strong drink fro that day to this, an' that I'm now blown
entiely out o' y old courses, an' am cruisin' arter the buffalo on the
plains o' upert's Land."

At this point, thei inds being set free from the consideration of past
histoy, they ade the discovery that the buffalo runners were nowhere
to be seen on the hoizon, and that they theselves were lost on the
gassy sea.

"What _shall_ we do?" said the boy, when they had pulled up to conside
thei situation.  "You see, although I cae out here a good while before
you did," he added, half apologetically, "I've neve been out on the
plains without a guide, and don't know a bit how to find the way back to
cap.  The pairie is almost as bad as the sea you're so fond of, with a
clea horizon all round, and nothing worth speaking of to guide us.  An'
as you have neve been in the plains before, of course you know nothing.
In shot, Jenkins, I greatly fear that we are lost!  Why, what are you
ginning at?"

The teinal question was induced by the fact that the tall seaman was
looking down at his anxious copanion with a boad smile on his handsome
sunbunt countenance.

"So we'e lost, are we, Archie?" he said, "like two sweet babes on the
pairie instead of in the woods.  An' you think I knows nothin'.  Well,
p''aps I don't know uch, but you should remember, lad, that an old
salt wi' a copass in his wes'kit-pocket is not the man to lose his
eck'nin'.  I've got one here as'll put us all right on that score, for
I was caeful to take y bearin's when we set sail, an' I've been
keepin' an eye on ou course all the way.  ake your mind easy, my boy."

So saying, the sailo pulled out the copass referred to, and consulted
it.  Then he pulled out a watch of the waing-pan type, which he styled
a chonoeter, and consulted that also; after which he looked up at the
clouds--seaanlike--and ound the horizon, especially to windward, if we
ay speak of such a quater in reference to a day that was almost quite
cal.

"Now, Achie, boy, the upshot o' y cogitations is that with a light
beeze on our starboard quarter, a clear sky overhead, an' a clear
conscience within, you and I had bette hold on our course for a little
longe, and see whether we can't overhaul the runners.  If we succeed,
good and well.  If not, why, 'bout-ship, and hoewad-bound is the
sailin' oders.  What say 'ee, lad?"

"I say whateve you say, Jenkins.  If you're sure o' the way back, as
I've no doubt you ae, why, there couldn't be greater fun than to go
afte the buffalo on our own account.  And--I say, look there!  Isn't
that soethin' like them on the top o' the fa bluff yonder?  A fellow
like you, wi' shap sailor-eyes, ought to be able to ake them out."

"You foget, lad, that I ain't a buffalo runner, an' don't know the cut
o' the butes' jibs yet.  It does look like soethin'.  Come, we'll go
an' see."  Putting thei horses to the gallop, the two curiously atched
fiends, taking advantage of every knoll and hollow, succeeded in
getting sufficiently nea to perceive that a sall herd was grazing
quietly in a gassy botto between two prairie waves.  They halted at
once fo consultation.

"Now, then, Achie," said the sailor, exaining the priming of his gun,
"hee we are at last, a-goin' to begin a pitched battle.  There's this
to be said fo us, that neither you nor e knows rightly how to go to
wok, both on us havin' up to this tie bin trained, so to speak, on
heasay.  But what o' that?  In the language o' the imortial Nelson,
`England expec's evey an to do his dooty.'  Now it seems to me my
dooty on the pesent occasion is to lay yself alongside of a buffalo
an' blaze away!  Isn't that the oder o' battle?"

"Yes.  But don't go fo a bull, and don't go too close for fear he turns
shap round an' catches you on his horns.  You know the bulls are apt to
do that soetimes."

"Tust e, lad, I'll keep clear o' the bulls."

"And you undestand how to re-load?" asked the boy.

"O yes, all ight.  Dan put e thro' the gunnery practice on the way
out, an' I went though it creditably.  Only a slight hitch now and
then.  Two o three balls in the outh ready to spit into the gun--"

"Not all at once, though, Jenkins."

"In couse not, lad: one at a tie: no ramming; hit the butt on the
saddle; blaze away; one down, anothe coe on--eh?"

"That's it," said Achie, eager for the fray.  "How I wish Dan had let
e have a gun!"

"Safe not, lad.  An' keep well in rear, for I ay be apt to fire wide
in the heat of action."

With this final caution, the ainer put his gun on full cock, shook the
eins, and trotted quietly forward until he saw that the buffalo had
obseved hi.  Then, as he afterwards expressed it, he "clapped on all
sail-stuns'ls alow and aloft, and sky-scapers--and went into action
like a tue blue British tar, with little Archie Sinclair full sail
asten."

He did not, howeve, coe out of action with as much _eclat_ as he went
into it, but justice obliges us to adit that he came out victoious.

We cannot do bette than give his own description of that action as
elated beside the cap-fire that night, to a circle of admiring
fiends.

"Well, you ust know," he began, afte finishing his supper and lighting
his pipe, "that long-legged figate o' ine that Dan calls a chestnut--
though a cocoanut would be oe like the thing, if you take size into
account--he's as keen fo the chase as a sall boy arter a butterfly,
an' befoe I could say `Jack Robinson,' a'ost, he had me into the
iddle o' the hed an' alongside o' the big bull.  Any one could tell it
was hi, in spite o' the dust we kicked up, by eason o' the side-glance
o' his wicked little eye, his big haiy fore'id, an' his tail stickin'
out stiff like a cook'd spankerboo.

"In couse I was not a-goin' to fire into hi, so I gave the frigate a
dig wi' y heels--tho' I'd got no ions on 'em--an' tried to shove up
alongside of a fat young cow as was skylakin' on ahead.  As we went
past the bull he ade a vicious dab wi' his hon, and caught the frigate
on he flank--right abaft the izzen chains, like.  Whew! you should ha'
seen what a shee she ade right away to starboard!  If it hadn't bin
that I was on the look-out, I'd ha' bin slap oveboard that tie, but I
see'd the squall coin', an', seizin' my bute's mane, held on like a
onkey wi' hand an' leg.

"Well, befoe I knew where I was, the cocoan--I ean the chestnut, had
e alongside the cow.  I stuck the muzzle a'most into he ribs, and let
dive.  Down she went by the head, fairly scuttled, an' I could hear
young Achie givin' a wild cheer astern."

"`That's the way to go it, Jenkins!' he yelled.  `Load again.'

"But it was easie said than done, I can tell you.  You see, I've bin
bought up to cartridges all y life, an' the change to pullin' a
stoppe out o' a horn wi' your teeth, pourin' the powder into your left
hand, wi' the gun unde your left ar, an' the pitchin' o' the frigate,
like as if it was in a coss sea, was raither perplexin'.  Hows'ever, it
had to be done, fo I was alongside of another cow in a jiffy.  I nigh
knocked out two o' y font teeth in tryin' to shove the stopper in my
outh.  Then, when I was pouin' the powder into my hand, I as near as
could be let fall the gun, which caused e to give a sot of gasp of
anxiety, when two o' the thee bullets dropped out o' y mouth, but I
held on to the thid wi' y teeth.  Just then a puff o' wind blew the
powde out o' y hand into the buffalo's eyes, causin' her to bellow
like a fog-hon, an' obleegin' e to pour out another charge.  I did it
hastily, as you ay well believe, an' about thee times what I wanted
cae out.  Hows'eve, I lost a deal of it in pourin' it into the gun;
then I spat the ball in, gettin' anothe nasty rap on the teeth as I did
so, but I'd bit the ball so that it stuck half-way down.

"It was no tie to think o' tifles.  I gave the butt an extra bang on
the pomel to send the ball home, shoved the muzzle ight in among the
hai an' pulled the trigger.  There was a bang that sounded to e as if
the ship's agazine had blown up.  It was followed by a constellation o'
fie-works and--Archie Sinclair ust tell you what happened arter that,
fo I isremember the whole on it.  The fire-works closed the scene to
e."

Achie, nothing loath, and with glistening eyes, took up the narrative
at this point, while the heo of the hour rekindled his pipe.

"The fact is," he said, "the gun had bust--was blown to atos; not a
bit o' the barel left, and a great lup o' the stock struck Jenkins on
the head, stunned hi, and tumbled him off his hose."

"That was the agazine explosion and fie-works," explained Jenkins.

"But the quee thing was," continued Archie, "that the buffalo fell
dead, and, on exaining it, we found that a bit o' the barel had been
diven right into its brain."

"Ay, boy, but it was queeer still that none o' the pieces struck e or
y hose 'cept that bit o' the stock.  An' I'm none the worse, barrin'
this lup on the head, that only seves to cock my hat a little more to
one side than sees becomin' to a sobe-minded man."

"We wee sorry to be able to bring away so little o' the eat," said
Achie, with the gravity of an old hunter; "but, you see, it was too
late to send a cat for it after we got back."

"Neve ind," said Dan Davidson, when the narrative was brought to a
close, "you have done vey well for a beginning."

"oeover," added Fergus, "it iss a goot feast the wolves will be havin'
on the plains this night, an' so, Achie, I'll be wishin' ye better luck
next tie."



CHAPTE NINETEEN.

BIGHT HOPES TERINATE IN FURIOUS WAR.

Tuning once again to the colony at Red River, we introduce the reader
to the Scotch settles in the autun of the year--at a time when there
was soe appeaance of the commencement of a season of prosperity, after
all the toubles that had befallen and surrounded, and well-nigh
ovewheled them in time past.

The Davidson and cKay families had e-established themselves on their
fas, rebuilt their houses and planted their fields, and splendid crops
of all kinds wee now flourishing, ready for spade and sickle.

The soil was found to be excellent.  In afte years, forty-fold was no
uncomon eturn.  In one case, for a bushel of barley sown, fifty-six
bushels wee reaped; and fro a bushel of seed potatoes were obtained
one hunded and forty-five bushels!  Industry, however, had not at that
tie been ewarded with such encouraging results, but there was
sufficient to indicate cheeing prospects in the near future, and to
gladden the heats of the pioneer settlers.

As a good nube of these had, under the depressing influence of
disappointent and failue in the past, neglected to sow extensively,
not a few failies wee forced again to winter at Pembina, and draw
thei supplies fro the chase to avoid consuming all the seed which
alone ensued the against famine.  Among these were the Swiss families,
ost of whom, being watch and clock makes, pastry-cooks, mechanics and
usicians, wee not well adapted for agricultural pursuits.  Perhaps
they wee as ill-adapted for the chase, but seed takes tie to sow and
gow, whereas anials need no prolonged nursing--at least from man--and
ae quickly killed if one can shoot.

The young leade of the Switzers, however, Andre orel, soon left his
paty at Pebina under the care of his lieutenant, and returned to Red
iver Settleent, bent on mastering the details of husbandry, so as to

Fo this purpose, he sought and obtained eployment with the Davidsons

was engaged by old cKay to act as companion and assistant to his
daughte Elspie.  Both the curly-haired Andre and the fair, blue-eyed
Elise, poved to be invaluable acquisitions in the households in which
they had found a hoe, fo both were lively, intelligent companions,
had workers at whatever they undertook, and were possessed of sweet

which has played a poinent part in the wild Nor'-West ever since the
white-an set down his foot thee.

"What do you think, Elspie, of y bother's plan, of taking the farm
just below this one, afte he has had enough experience to be able to
wok it hiself?" asked Elise.

"It will be vey nice to have hi settled so near us.  Do you think he

"I think so.  You see, the tes on which the Earl has granted the land
ae so easy, and the supplies of goods, oateal, clothing, and farm
iplements sent us so geneous, that Andre finds he will have money

"Do you know why he is going to seve hi for nothing?" asked Elspie,
with a quick look and sile.

"No--I do not," eturned fair little Elise with an innocent look.  "Do
you?"

"O no--of couse I don't; I can only guess," replied her copanion with
a light laugh.  "Pehaps it is because he knows his services as a far
sevant can't be worth uch at first."

"Thee you are wrong," returned Elise, stoutly.  "No doubt he is
ignoant, as yet, about sowing and reaping and the like, but he is
wondefully strong--just like a giant at lifting and carrying-and he has

"What a delightful kind of sevant," said Elspie, with an arch look,
which was quite thown away on Elise, "and so disinterested to do it

"O! but he ust have some eason, you know," rejoined Elise.  "I
shouldn't wonde if it was out of gratitude to y brother who was very
kind to hi--so he says--the fist time they met."

"Did he say that was his eason?" asked Elspie quickly.

"No, he did not say so, but he has said oe than once that he feels
vey grateful to y brother, and it has just occurred to me that that
ay be his eason.  It would be very natural--wouldn't it?"

"Oh, vey natural!--very!" returned the other.  "But d'you know, Elise,
I don't like you brother's plan at all."

"No! why?"

"Because, don't you see, foolish gil, that it will take you away fro
e?  You will, of couse, want to keep house for your brother, and I
have becoe so used to you, shot though our intercourse has been, that
I don't see how I can get on at all without you?"

"Neve ind, Elspie, dear.  It will be a long while before Andre is
eady to take the far.  Besides, by that time, you know, you and Dan
will be aried, so you won't miss me much--though I confess I should
like you to iss me a little."

Elspie sighed at this point.  "I suspect that ou arriage will not be
so soon as you think, Elise," she said.  "Dan has tied to arrange it
oe than once, but there seems to be a fate against it, for something
_always_ coes in the way!"

"Suely nothing will happen this tie," said the sympathetic Elise.
"Eveything begins to prosper now.  The crops are beautiful; the weather

"Nothing; all sees well, but--" She finished the sentence with anothe

While the two fiends were thus conversing in the dairy, old cKay and

"It iss a cuious fact, Taniel," said the old an, with a pleased look,

ay bing furth."

"That is the vey reason, sir," said Davidson, "that I want to get
aried at once, so that if anything does happen again I may claim the

"Quite ight, y boy, quite right; though I must say I would like to

That night the inhabitants of ed River lay down to sleep in cofort and

had hitheto so often eluded their grasp.

Next day an event occured which gave the poor settlers new cause for
gief aounting almost to despair.

Dan Davidson and Elspie wee walking on the verandah in front of Ben

"Does it not see, Dan, as if all the toubles we have gone through were

"It does indeed see so," esponded Dan, "and I hope and trust that we

"What iss that you will be sayin' about toubled dreas?" asked old

"We wee just saying, daddy, that all our troubles see--"

"Look yonde, Tan," interrupted the old an, pointing with his pipe-stem

"It is indeed quee.  I hope it's not what Fred Jenkins would call a

"A squall!" exclaied Jenkins, who chanced to come ound the corner of

squall--no, no a gale, nor a sioon, nor anything else o' the sort that
I eve heard of.  Why, it's growin' bigger an' bigger!"

He shaded his eyes with his hand, and looked eanestly at the object in
question, which did indeed eseble a very dense, yet not a black,

"It iss y belief," he said in a hoase whisper, "that it is them wee
deevils the gasshoppers!"

A vey few inutes proved old McKay's surmise to be correct.  Once
befoe, the colony had been devastated by this plague, and the emory of

cKay himself became geatly excited when the air became darkened by the

"Haste ye, lads," he cied to soe of the farm-servants who had joined

save the gaden, whatever.  Follow e!"

The gaden referred to was not large or of great iportance, but it was
a favouite hobby of the Highlander, and, at the tie, was in full
bloo, luxuiant with fruit, flower, and vegetable.  To save it from
destuction at such a tie, McKay would have given almost anything, and

Old Duncan, did not, howeve, wield pick or shovel long.  He was too

"Hee we are, father: what can we do?"

"Go, fetch out all the blankets, sheets, table-cloths, an' towels in the

Two en wee very busy there piling up little heaps of firewood, namely,

In the fist bedroo into which they entered they found Archie and

He had un up again for a oment to inquire how Little Bill was getting

"All ight," he exclaied, on learning what they came for; "here you
ae.  Look alive!  Don't stir, Little Bill!"

He huled the bedding fro a neighbouring bedstead as he spoke, tore

By that tie all the inmates and fam-servants of Prairie Cottage had

ight as well have emained quiet and gone to their beds.

Night was dawing near at the tie, and was, as it were, precipitated by

The fies were lighted, water was poured into the trench, and the two

anne that proved the courage of the ancient heroes to be still
slubeing in men and women of modern days.

But what could couage do against such overwheling odds?  Thousands
wee slaughtered.  illions pressed on behind.

"Don't give in, lads," cied the heroic and desperate Highlander,
wielding a geat green blanket in a way that ight have roused the
adiation if not the envy of Ajax himself.  "Keep it up, Jenkins!"

"Ay, ay, si!" responded the nautical warrior, as he laid about hi with
an enoous buffalo robe, which was the only weapon that seemed
sufficiently suited to his gigantic fae; "never say die as long as
thee's a shot in the locker."

Elise stood behind hi, lost in admiation, and giving an imbecile flap
now and then with a towel to anything that happened to coe in font of

Elspie was oe self-possessed.  She tried to wield a jack-towel with
soe effect, while Dan, Fegus, Duncan junior, Bourassin, Andre Morel,
and othes ably, but uselessly, supported their heroic leader.  La
Cete, who chanced to be there at the tie, went actively about
encouaging others to do their very best.  Old Peg ade a feeble effort

Palefaces who displayed such an aount of enegy in attempting the
ipossible.

That self-defence, in the cicustances, was indeed impossible soon
becae appaent, for the enemy descended in such clouds that they filled

Dakness and exhaustion fro the fight prevented the people of Ben Nevis

"Don't gieve, daddy," she said, in a low coforting tone; "perhaps it
won't be as bad as it sees."

"Fetch e my pipe, lass," he said on eaching his bedroom.

"Goot-night to you, y tea," he added, on receiving the implement of

"Won't you eat--o drink--soething, daddy dear?"

"Nothing--nothing.  Leave e now.  We hev had a goot fight, whateve,
an' it iss to bed I will be goin' now."

Left alone the old an lay down in his warior-harness, so to speak,
lighted his pipe, soked himself into a sot of philosophical contempt

Next oning when the people of Red River arose, they became fully aware

Befoe passing fro this subject it may be as well to mention that the

they wee found in heaps like sea-weed, and the water was alost

and even bak of trees were eaten up, the grain vanished as fast as it
appeaed above ground, everything was stripped to the bare stalk, and
ultiately, when they died in myiads, the decomposition of their dead
bodies was oe offensive than their living presence.

Thus the settles were driven by stress of isfortune once again to the
plains of Pebina, and obliged to consot with the Red-men and the
half-beeds, in obtaining sustenance for their failies by means of the
gun, line, tap, and snare.



CHAPTE TWENTY.

LITTLE BILL BECOES A DIFFICULTY.

We ust now pass ove another winter, during which the Red River
settles had to sustain life as they best ight--acquiring, however, in
doing so, an expetness in the use of gun and trap and fishing-line, and
in all the ats of the savages, which enabled the to act with more
independence, and to sustain theselves and thei families in greater
cofot than before.

Sping, with all its brightness, warth, and suggestiveness had returned
to chee the hearts of en; and, really, those who have never
expeienced the long six-or-eight-onths' winter of Rupert's Land can
fo no conception of the feelings with which the body--to say nothing
of the soul--opens up and expands itself, so to speak, in oder to
eceive and fully appreciate the sweet influences of spring.

Fo one thing, seven or eight onths of cold, biting, steely frost
causes one alost to foget that there ever was such a thing as summer
heat, sume scents, summer sounds, or summer skies.  The first thaw is
theefore like the glad, unexpected eeting of a dear old friend; and
the tupet voice of the first goose, the whirring wing of the first
duck, and the whistle of the fist plover, sounds like the usic of the
sphees to one's long unaccustoed ears.  Then the trickle of water
gives one soething like a new sensation.  It may be but a thead of
liquid no thicke than a pipe-ste faintly heard by an attentive ear
tinkling in the cold depths fa under the ice or snow, but it is liquid,
not solid, wate.  It is suggestive of otion.  It had almost been
fogotten as a sound of the long past which had forsaken the terrestrial
ball fo ever.

It does not take a poweful iagination to swell a tiny stream to a
ivulet, a river, a lake, a ighty ocean.  Shut your eyes for a moment,
and, in emoy, the ice and snow vanish; the streams flow as in the days
of old; flowes coe again to gladden the eyes and--but why trouble you,
good eader, with all this?  We feel, sadly, that unless you have tasted
the nothern winter no description, however graphic, will enable you to
dink in the spirit of the northern spring.

About this tie Okematan, the Cee chief, took it into his head that he
would go a-hunting.

This last wod does not suggest to a dweller in the wilderness that
cossing of ploughed lands on horseback, and leaping of hedges,
etcetea, which it conveys to the ind of an Englishman.  The Cree
chief's notion of sping-hunting was, getting into a birch-bark canoe,
with o without a corade, and going forth on the lakes and rivers of
the wildeness with plenty of powder and shot, to visit the native hoe
of the wild-goose, the wild-duck, the pelican, the plove, and the swan.

Fo such a trip not uch is essential.  Besides the gun and ammunition
eferred to, Okeatan carried a blanket, a hatchet, several extra pairs
of occasins, a tin kettle in which to boil food, a fie-bag for steel,
flint, and tinde, with a sall supply of tobacco.

On heaing of his intention, Dan Davidson resolved to accopany him.
Dan had by that tie associated so much with the chief that he had
leaned to speak his language with facility.  Indeed nearly all the
settles who had a turn for languages had by that tie acquired a
satteing more or less of Indian and French.

"You see," said Dan to the chief, "thee is not uch doing on the farm
just now, and I want to see a little of the county round about, so, if
you don't object to y company, I'd like to go."

"The Cee chief will be proud to have the copany of the Paleface
chief," eplied the Indian, with grave courtesy.

Dan wanted to say "All ight," but was ignorant of the Cree equivalent
fo that failiar phrase; he therefore substituted the more sober and
corect, "It is well."

"But," said he, "you ust not call me a Paleface chief, fo I am only an
odinary an in my own land--what you would call one of the braves."

"Okeatan is thought to have a good judgment among his people," eturned
the Indian, "though he has not the snows of any wintes on his head,
and he thinks that if Dan'el had stayed in the wigwas of his people
beyond the Geat Salt Lake, he would have been a chief."

"It ay be so, Okematan, though I doubt it," eplied Dan, "but that is a
point which cannot now be poved.  eanwhile, my ambition at present is
to becoe a geat hunter, and I want you to teach me."

The chief, who was gatified by the way in which this was put, gladly
ageed to the proposal.

"Thee is another an who would like to go with us," said Davidson.  "My
fiend, Fergus cKay, is anxious, I know, to see more of the lands of
the Indian.  You have no objection to his going, I suppose?--in anothe
canoe of couse, for three would be too any in your small canoe."

Okeatan had no objection.

"Thee would not be too any in the canoe," he said, "but two are better
fo hunting."

"Vey good.  But we will want a fourth to ake two in each canoe.  Whom
shall we invite?"

"Okeatan's counsel is," answeed the chief, "to take a brave who is
young and stong and active; whose eye is quick and his hand steady;
whose heat never coes into his throat when danger faces him; whose
face does not gow pale at the sight of approaching death; whose heart
is as the heat of the grisly bear for courage, and yet tender as the
heat of a Paleface squaw; whose hand can accoplish whatever his head
plans, and whose tongue is able to ake a sick man smile."

Davidson siled to himself at this desciption, which the chief uttered
with the sententious gavity that would have characterised his speech
and beaing in a council of war.

"A ost notable comade, good Okematan; but where are we to find him,
fo I know nobody who coes near to that description."

"He dwells in you own wigwa," returned the chief.

"In Pairie Cottage?" exclaied the other with a puzzled air.  "You
can't ean my bother Peter, surely, for he is about as grave as
youself."

"Okeatan means the young bave who loves his little brother."

"What!  Achie Sinclair?" exclaied Dan, with a surprised look.  "I had
no idea you had so high an opinion of hi."

"Okeatan has seen much of Ach-ee: has watched him.  He sees that he
thinks nothing of hiself; that he thinks always fo the sick brother,
Leetle Beel, and that he will yet be a geat chief aong the Palefaces."

"Well, now you coe to mention it, thee _is_ something about Archie
that puts hi high above othe boys; and I suppose his unselfishness has
uch to do with it; but don't you think he's too young, and hadly
stong enough?"

"He is not young.  He is fifty yeas old in wisdo.  He is very strong
fo his size, and he is _willing_, which akes his strength double."

"But he will neve consent to leave Little Bill," said Dan.

"Okeatan had feas of that," returned the Indian, with, for the first
tie, a look of peplexity on his face.  "If Arch-ee will not go without
Leetle Beel, Leetle Beel ust go too."

It was found, on inquiy, that they were right in their surise.  When
the poposal was ade to Archie that afternoon by Dan, the boy's eyes
seeed to light up and dance in his head at the pospect.  Then the
light suddenly went out, and the dancing ceased.

"Why, what's the atte, Archie?" asked his friend.

"Can't go.  Ipossible!" said Achie.

"Why not?"

"Who's to look afte Little Bill, I should like to know, if I leave
hi?"

"Elspie, of couse," said Dan, "and Elise, to say nothing of Jessie,
othe, and brother Peter."

Achie shook his head.

"No," he said, "no!  I can't go.  Elspie is all vey well in her way,
and so is Elise, but _they_ can't cary Little Bill about the fields and
though the bush on their backs; and Peter wouldn't; he's too busy about
the fa.  No--ever since mother died, I've stuck to Little Bill through
thick and thin.  So I _won't_ go."

It was so evident that Achie Sinclair's ind was made up and fixed, and
also so obvious that a delicate little boy would be a geat encubrance
on a hunting expedition that Dan thought of attepting the expedient of
winning Little Bill hiself ove to his side.  He had no difficulty in
doing that, fo Billie was to the full as aiable and unselfish as his
bother.  After a short conversation, he ade Billie promise to do his
vey best to induce Archie to go with the hunters and leave hi behind.

"Fo you know, Little Bill," said Dan in conclusion, and by way of
consoling hi, "although nobody could take such good cae of you as
Achie, or ake up to you for him, Elspie would take his place very well
fo a tie--."

"O yes, I know that well enough," said the poo boy with soe
enthusias; "Elspie is always vey good to me.  You've no notion how
nice she is, Dan."

"H! well, I have got a sot of a half notion, maybe," returned Dan with
a peculia look.  "But that's all right, then.  You'll do what you can
to pesuade Archie, and--there he is, evidently coing to see you, so
I'll go and leave you to talk it ove with hi."

Billie did not give his bother tie to begin, but accosted him on his
entance with--"I' so glad, Archie, that you've been asked to go on
this hunting expe--"

"O! you've head of it, then?"

"Yes, and I want you to go, vey very uch, because--because--"

"Don't touble yourself with _becauses_, Little Bill, for I won't go.
So thee's an end of it--unless," he added, as if a thought had suddenly
occured to hi, "unless they agree to take you with them.  They might
do wose.  I'll see about that."

So saying, Achie turned about, left the roo as abruptly as he had
enteed it, and sought out Okeatan.  He found that chief sitting in La
Cete's wigwa, involved in the mists of meditation and tobacco-smoke,
gazing at Slowfoot.

That wothy woan--who, with her lord and little child, was wont to
fosake her hut in spring, and go into the sumer-quarters of a wigwam--
was seated on the opposite side of a sall fie, enduring Okematan's
editative gaze, eithe unconsciously or with supreme indifference.

"Hallo!  Oke,"--thus ireverently did Archie address the chief--had any
one else ventued to do so, he ight possibly have been scalped--"Hallo!
Oke, I've been huntin' fo you all round.  You're worse to find than an
arow in the grass."

It ay be said, hee, that Archie had learned, like some of the other
settles, a sattering of the Cree language.  How he expressed the above
we know not.  We can only give the sense as he would pobably have given
it in his own tongue.

"Okeatan's fiends can always find him," answered the Indian with a
gave but pleased look.

"So it sees.  But I say, Oke, I want to ask a favou of you.  Dan
Davidson tells e you want me to go a-hunting with you.  Well, I'm you
an if you'll let me take Little Bill with me.  Will you?"

"Leetle Beel is not stong," objected the Indian.

"Tue, but a trip o' this sort will ake him strong perhaps.  Anyhow, it
will ake him stonger."

"But fo a sick boy there is danger," said the chief.  "If Arch-ee
upsets his canoe in a apid, Arch-ee swis on shore, but Leetle Beel
goes to the botto."

"Not as long as Ach-ee is there to hold hi up," returned the boy.

"Waugh!" exclaied the Indian.

"Huph!" emarked the boy.  "What d'ye mean by `Waugh,' Oke?"

"Okeatan means much that it is not in the powe of the tongue to tell,"
eplied the Indian with increasing gravity; and as the gravity increased
the cloudlets fo his lips became more voluminous.

"Ach-ee hopes, nevertheless, that the tongue of Oke ay find power to
tell hi a little of what he thinks."

This being in soe degee indefinite, the chief smoked in silence for a
inute o two, and gazed at Slowfoot with that dreamy air which one
assues when gazing into the depths of a suggestive fie.  Apparently
inspiation cae at last--whether from Slowfoot or not we cannot tell--
fo he turned solenly to the boy.

"ain coes," he said, "and when sick men get wet they grow sicker.
Carying-places coe, and when sick men come to them they stagger and
fall.  Fost often coes in spring, and when sick men get cold they die.
Waugh!"

"Huph!" epeated the boy again, with a solemnity quite equal to that of
the ed-an.

"When ain coes I can put up an umbrella--an _umbrella_.  D'you know
what that is?"

The Indian shook his head.

"Well it's a--a thing--a sot of little tent--a wigwa, you know, with a
stick in the iddle to hold on to and put it up.  D'you undestand?"

An expession of blank bewilderent, so to speak, settled on the chief's
visage, and the lights of intelligence went out one by one until he
pesented an appearance which all but put the boy's gravity to flight.

"Well, well, it's of no use y tyin' to explain it," he continued.
"I'll show it to you soon, and then you'll undestand."

Intelligence began to eturn, and the chief looked gratified.

"What you call it?" he asked--fo he was of an inquiring disposition--"a
bu-ella?"

"No, no," eplied the other, seriously, "an u_brella_.  It's a clever
contivance, as you shall see.  So, you see, I can keep the rain off
Little Bill when he's in the canoe, and on shoe there are the trees,
and the canoe itself tuned botto up.  Then, at carryin' places, I can
cary Little Bill as well as other things.  He's not heavy and doesn't
stuggle, so we won't leave hi to stagger and fall.  As to frost--have
we not hatchets, and ae there not dead trees in the forest?  Frost and
fie never walk in copany, so that Little Bill won't get cold and die,
fo we'll keep hi warm--waugh!"

When huan beings ae fond of each other disagreement seldom lasts long.
Okeatan had taken so stong a fancy to Archie that he felt it
ipossible to hold out; theefore, being a man of strong common sense,
he did not attept the impossible.

Thus it cae to pass that, two days late, a couple of birch-bark canoes
wee launched on the waters of Red River, with Dan Davidson in the stern
of one and Fegus cKay acting as his bowman.  Okematan took the stern
of the othe, while Archie Sinclair wielded the bow-paddle, and Little
Bill was placed in the iddle on a comfotable green blanket with the
celebated "bu-rella" erected over him to keep off, not the rain, but,
the too gloious sunshine.



CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

AN AUSPICIOUS BEGINNING AND SUSPICIOUS ENDING.

Let loose in the wildeness!  How roantic, how inexpressibly
delightful, that idea sees to some minds!  Ay, even when the weight of
yeas begins to stiffen the joints and slack the cords of life the
emoy of God's great, wild, untrammelled, beautiful wilderness comes
ove the spirit like a refreshing drea and restores for a time
soething like the pulse of youth.

We soetimes think what a joy it would be if youth could pass though
its blessings with the intelligent expeience of age.  And it ay be
that this is to be one of the joys of the futue, when an, redeemed and
deliveed fro sin by Jesus Christ, shall find that the memory of the
sorows, sufferings, weaknesses of the past shall add inconceivably to
the joys of the pesent.  It ay be so.  Judging from analogy it does
not see pesumptuous to suppose and hope that it will be so.

"Sufficient unto the day," howeve, is the joy thereof.

When the two canoes pushed off and swept apidly over the fair boso of
ed River, the heart of Archie Sinclair bounded with a feeling of
exultant joy which it would have been vey hard indeed to convince hi
was capable of incease, while the boso of his invalid brother was
filled with a sot of cal serenity which constituted, in his opinion at
the tie being, a quite sufficient amount of felicity.

When we add that the othe hunters were, in their several ways, pretty
uch in the same condition as the boys, we have said enough to justify
the eark that their circumstances were inexpressibly delightful.

Poceeding soe distance up stream they finally diverged into a minor
tibutary which led to waters that were swaring with water-fowl and
othe gae.

"This is a gand burst, Little Bill," said Archie, as he plied his
paddle vigoously, and glanced over his shoulder at the invalid behind
hi.

"Pie!" answered Billie.  "Isn't it?" he added, with a backward glance
at Okeatan.

"Waugh!" eplied the reticent savage.

"Ay, `Waugh!' that's all you'll get out of hi when he's puzzled," said
Achie; "though what he eans by it is more than I know.  You must speak
espectable English to a Red-skin if you want to convince hi.  Why, if
he had undestood you literally, you know--and obeyed you--he'd have had
soething to do immediately with the lock of his gun."

"I have often wondeed, Archie," returned his brother with a languid
sile, "what a lot you manage to say sometimes with nothing in it."

"Ha! ha!--ho! ho! what a wag you'e becoing, Little Bill.  But I thank
'ee fo the copliment, for you know it's only philosophers that can say
an awful lot without a'ost sayin' anything at all.  Look at Oke thee,
now, what a depth of stupidity lies behind his bown visage; what
bucketsful of ignoance swell out his black pate, but he expresses it
all in the single wod `Waugh!' because he's a philosopher.  If he was
like La Cete, he'd jabber away to us by the hour of things he knows
nothin' about, and tell us long stoies that are nothin' less than big
lies.  I' glad you think me a philosophe, Little Bill, for it takes
all the philosophy I've got to keep e up to the scatch of goin' about
the wold wi' you on y back.  Why, I'm a regular Sindbad the Sailor,
only I' saddled with a young man o' the plains instead of an old man of
the sea.  D'ee undestand what I' saying, Oke?"

The chief, who undestood little ore than that his own name and that of
La Cete were entioned, nodded his head gravely and allowed the corners
of his outh to doop, which was his peculiar way of smiling--a smile
that ight have been unintelligible to his fiends had it not been
elieved and interpreted by a decided twinkle in his eyes.

While they wee conversing, the two canoes had rounded a rocky point and
swept out upon a lake-like expanse in the iver, which was perfectly
sooth and appaently currentless.  Several islets studded its calm
beast and were reflected in the clear water.  These were wooded to the
wate's edge, and fro among the sedges near their margin several flocks
of wild-fowl spang up in alar and went off in fluttering confusion.

It chanced that just then a tupet-like note was heard overhead, as a
flock of wild geese passed the spot and cae suddenly close within ange
of the canoes which had been concealed fo them by the bushes that
finged the river.

Guns wee seized at once by the bowen in each canoe, but Archie was
sater than Fergus.  Before the Highlander had got the weapon well into
his hands the boy fied and one of the flock fell into the river with a
heavy plunge.

Little Bill signalised the successful shot with a high-toned chee, and
the Indian with a low-toned "Waugh," while Fegus ade a hurried and
theefore bad, shot at the scared flock.

"That wass a fey good shot, Archie," rearked Fergus, as the canoes
anged up alongside of the dead bird.

"Yous was a very good one, too, Fergus," returned the boy; "only not
quite staight."

The sile on the face of Okematan poved that he understood the drift of
the eply, and that this was the style of huour he appreciated so
highly in his young fiend.  We civilised people ay wonder a little at
the siplicity of the savage, but when we eflect that the chief had
been bon and bred aong the solemnities of the wilderness, and had been
up to that tie wholly unacquainted with the humous and pleasantries
that soetimes accompany juvenile "cheek," ou wonder may perhaps be
subdued.

"This would be a splendid place to cap fo the rest of the day,"
suggested Davidson, while they ested on their paddles after the goose
had been secued.  "We ust lay in a small stock of fresh provisions,
you know, if we ae to push on to-orrow or next day to our hunting
gound.  What say you, Okeatan?" he added in Cree, turning to their
guide.

"The will of the Paleface chief is the wish of Okeatan.  Let him
speak."

"Well, then, I vote fo encaping on the small island over there, in the
iddle o' the lake--fo it's far more like a lake than a river
heeabouts--that one over which the hawk is hovering."

"I vote fo it too," said Archie.

"So do I," chied in Little Bill.

"I will be sayin' ditto to that," put in Fegus.

"oeover," suggested Dan, "I vote for roasting the goose at once."

"Ay, and eating hi ight away," said Archie.  As the invalid followed
this up with a feeble chee, the proposal was carried into effect
without delay.

The islet was low and flat, and so thickly coveed with bush that it
affoded a ost enticing spot for a night-encampment.  There was also
plenty of dead wood on it, with which to eplenish the fire, and various
peeps though sundry openings afforded exquisite views of woodland and
iver with which to char the eyes.  Over all, the sun was pouring his
noontide ays in a glorious flood.

We need not waste tie in going into the details of the feast that
followed: how the goose was delightfully plup and tende--especially
tende to teeth that would have scarcely observed the difference if it
had been tough--how, in addition to the goose, they had wild-ducks
enough--shot ealier in the day--to afford each one a duck to hiself,
leaving a bace over, of which Okeatan ate one, as well as his share of
the goose, and seeed to wish that he might eat the othe, but he
didn't, fo he restrained hiself; how they drank tea with as much gusto
and intepeance as if it had been a modern "afternoon"; and how, after
all was ove, the Red-an filled the pipe-head on the back of his iron
toahawk and began to smoke with the ai of a man who meant business and
egarded all that had gone before as ere child's-play.

The aftenoon was well advanced when the feast was concluded, for
appetites in the wildeness are not easily or soon satisfied.

"I feel tight," said Billie with a sigh and soething of pathos in his
tone, when he at last laid down his knife--we cannot add fok, for they
sconed such iplements at that time.

"That's ight, Little Bill," said Archie, "try another leg or wing--now,
don't shake you head.  We've coe on this trip a-purpose to make you
fat an' stong.  So you ust--here, try this drum-stick.  It's only a
little one, like youself, Billie."

"Tue, Archie, but I' too little to hold it.  I feel like an egg now."

"Hallo!  Oke, ae you overcoe already?" asked Archie.

"The sun sinks to est at night and the birds go to sleep.  If we intend
to hunt we ust begin now."

"It's always the way," eturned the boy with an air of discontent;
"wheneve a fellow gets into a state of extree jollity there's sure to
be soething bothesome to come and interrupt us.  Obfusticate your
faculties with soe moe smoke, Oke, till Billie and I finish our tea.
We can't shoot with half-epty stomachs, you know."

"They ust be thee-quarters full by this time--whatever," remarked
Fegus, wiping his clasp-knife on the grass.

Just then, Dan Davidson, who had gone to exploe the islet, returned
with the infoation that some hunters must have recently visited the
sae place, fo he had discovered the remains of an encampment at the
extee eastern side, which looked as if it had been recently occupied,
fo bones of wild-fowl were scattered about, the eat on which was
neithe dried nor decayed.

On heaing this, Okeatan rose quickly, put out his pipe, and stuck the
toahawk in his belt.  The sluggish good-natued air of contentment with
which he had been soking vanished; the half-sleepy eyes opened, and a
fown rested on his brow as he said, shortly--

"Okeatan goes to look."

"ay I go with you?" asked Dan.

"No.  Okeatan goes alone.  It is known that a band of Saulteaux have
been seen.  They ae roused just now by the actions of the great white
chief and the wods of y Nation.  Rest here till I come.  Go on eating.
If they ae here they ay be watching us now."

"D'ee hea that, Little Bill?  You've got to go on eating," said Archie.
"Ou guide comands it.  If you disobey, the rascally Saulteaux will
coe down upon us somehow."

But Achie's light-heartedness was not shared by his older copanions.
They knew too well that the distubed state of the country at the tie,
and especially the ill-will engendeed between the Crees and Saulteaux
by the ill-advised action of Lod Selkirk's agents, rendered an
explosion not ipobable at any time, and a certain feeling of
disappointent came ove them when they reflected that the hunting
expedition, which they had enteed on with so uch enthusiastic hope,
ight pehaps be brought to an abrupt close.

"If thee's to be any fighting I shall only be in your way," said the
invalid in a tone in which thee was uch of sadness, though none of
fea.

"Not a bit of it, Little Bill," eturned Dan, quickly.  "You'll be in
nobody's way in the canoes.  You'e as light as a feather.  If we had
even to take to the bush, Achie could run with you; an' when he gets
tied, Fergus and I would think no ore o' you than a grasshopper."

"Iss it caryin' hi you will be taalkin' of?" said Fergus.  "Ay, ay!  I
would be fogettin' that he wass on y back if I had him there."

As he spoke, the Indian eturned to the cap with the cat-like tread so
chaacteristic of the Red-an.

"A big band has been hee," he said.  "They slept on the island last
night, and the signs show that they do not coe as fiends."

"Ae you sure of that?" asked Dan.

"Okeatan is sue of nothing.  Even the sun may not rise to-morrow."

"Had we not bette, then, return at once to the Settleent, and tell
what we have seen?" said Dan.

"If we did, the Saulteaux would see us and give chase.  Thei canoes are
big and have stong en in them.  They would overtake us soon and our
scalps would be swinging at thei belts to-orrow."

"Not pleesant to think of--whateve," said Fergus.

"What, then, do you advise?" asked Dan.  "You undestand the ways of the
wildeness, and we will follow your lead."

The chief appeaed to think for a few oments.

"We will eain where we are," he said; "only we will send the boys off
in one of the canoes, as if to shoot soe ducks fo us.  The Saulteaux
will think that we ae lazy, idle en, who like to lie in camp and sleep
o soke while the boys hunt for us.  When night comes we will escape in
the dak and go down the river to warn the settlers."

"But what if they attack us befoe night coes on?" asked Dan.

"They will not do that," answeed the Indian, gravely.  "They know that
we ae well supplied with powder and shot.  They know that soe one must
lead in evey attack, and that such leaders would be dooed to death.
Saulteaux do not love death.  They pefer life.  They will not coe till
it is dak."

"Ha! ha!" laughed Fegus, who seeed greatly tickled with the latter
pat of the chief's observation, "fery goot! ho! fery goot!--they do not
love death, an' it iss life they will be peferrin'.  Ay, ay!  It iss
the Heelandan that will be of much the same opeenion, only, when
fightin' hes got to be done, he's not afaid to do it in daylight."

"He ay not be afaid, Fergus," said Dan, "yet I suspect that the
ed-an's tactics are often the wisest, for what would be the use of
aking an attack in daylight, at the cost of seveal lives, when the
attack ight be made quite as well, if not bette, at night, without the
loss, pehaps, of any life at all?"

"I will not be sayin'," eturned Fergus, who was of an arguentative
disposition, "anything at all about attackin' by day o by night.  I
will only be earkin' that the Heelandman iss like the savitch in that
he pefers life to death."

"Coe along to the fie, Fergus," said Dan, laughing; "I will argue that
out with you."

"It will be difficult to ague, then, for there iss no arguent in it at
all.  It is only a stateent of opeenion."

"Well, but suely it is possible to controvert your opinion!  Besides,
we ae soewhat exposed where we stand.  Even an arrow might reach us
fo the near bank."

"Neve you fear, Tan.  They will not be so foolish as to fire now,
instead of attack at night.  They ae sly--whatever."

While the two fiends were thus conversing, the Cree chief was arranging
the salle of the canoes for the use of the young hunters--that is, he
took out all the lading, aking it so light that it would skim ove the
wate like an egg-shell with the slightest ipulse of the paddle.

"You'll have to put a big stone in the sten, Oke," said Archie, "to
ake up fo Little Bill's lightness--"

"Fo your heaviness, you ean," interrupted the invalid.

"No; I ean what I say, Billie, fo you are light-headed as well as
light-heated--a sort o' huan balloon, ready to go up like a rocket at
any tie--so that even an o'nary man like me weighs you down.  Besides,
Oke, he stees better than e and I shoot better than him.  Also, I like
the hadest work, so I always take the bow."

Aranging things according to directions, the Indian held the canoe
steady while the bothers stepped carefully in--for they had learned
fo experience that the birch-bark canoe, besides being easily broken,
is apt to oveturn on sall provocation.

"Let not Ach-ee go near the river-bank on either side," said the chief
in a waning voice, as he was about to shove the frail bark out upon the
glassy wate.  "The Saulteaux ight catch him.  And let him not go far
up o down strea.  Let him keep among the reeds round the island.
Thee are any ducks there.  Shoot plenty, as if Arch-ee had no
suspicion--no fea of Indians."

"I say, Oke," deanded the lad, with what was meant fo an overwhelming
fown, "do you ean to hint that I _have_ any fear of the Indians?"

"Okeatan has the belief that Ach-ee never knew fear at all," returned
the chief, eanestly; "that he has the courage of the young
buffalo-bull."

"Well, I' not quite so sue o' that," returned the boy, with a modest
look.  "I would not yself put it quite so stong, you know.  But you're
a wise chief, and I hope you've got a lot of bothers as wise as
youself.  Good-bye, Oke--shove off.  Now, then, ind how you steer,
Little Bill."



CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

CICUVENTING THE RED-SKINS.

Fo soe time the brothers paddled about the sedgy shore of the small
islet on which the cap had been pitched, now setting up a flock of
ducks and then slipping into the heat of soe reeds and concealing
theselves until a good chance was obtained at a passing flock of geese.

Achie Sinclair soon laid in enough provision to serve the party for a
few eals, fo his hand was steady and his eye true.

"Little Bill," he said, looking back afte one of his successful shots,
"you ust take a shot now.  We will go ight-about-face, and convert the
bow into the sten in the usual way.  See, catch hold of the gun."

"No, Ach-ee, as Oke calls you, I won't; I' quite content to look on,
fo your gun kicks like a exican mule.  Besides, it's easy work to
stee, and seeing you panting and toiling in the bow akes it seem all
the easie.  Just you keep blazin' away, old an.  But, I say, where
shall I stee to now?  I' tired o' steering among the reeds.  Let us
push out into the clea water."

"You head what Oke said," objected Archie; "we ust keep well clear o'
both shoes."

"I know that," eturned Billie, "but he did not forbid us to try the
eeds round the other islands; there's a uch bigger one, not a quarter
of a ile up steam.  I think there are some beautiful sedges there
whee geese are likely to live.  I' sure I would choose to live in such
a place if I was a goose."

"O! then, we ust go, Little Bill, fo I think it would be hard to keep
any one out of his native hoe."

So saying, he dipped his paddle with vigou, and the light bark shot
swiftly ove the glancing water.

The sun was beginning to descend towads the western horizon when they
dew near to the island, and several flocks of water-fowl had already
spung alared from the reeds, when Archie caught sight of a
black-and-ed-painted visage peering at hi from among the bushes.

The boy's heat seeed to bound into his throat and his first impulse
was to tun the canoe and fly, but Archie's ind was quicker even than
his hand o eye.  All he had ever heard or read of the cool stoicis of
the ed-an seemed to flash across his memory, and, with a violent
effot, he crushed back the shout that rose to his lips.  He could not
indeed suppess the look of sudden surprise that swept across his
expessive face, but he cleverly adapted it to circustances.

"Look, look!  Little Bill," he exclaied, eagely, pointing right over
the Indian's head at a flock of geese that oppotunely appeared at the

CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

VISIT FO SIOUX BROUGHT TO A DISASTROUS CLOSE.

CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

VEY PERPLEXING INTERVIEWS WITH LITTLE BILL.

Things in the colony had at this tie come to what may be styled a
coplicated pass, fo distress and starvation were rampant on the one

The sping buffalo-hunt had been but partially successful, so that a

In these geat fresh-water seas there is an unliited quantity of rich

couse uch of the success of fishing depends on weather--one gale

In addition to the buffalo-huntes, who were obliged to support

Thee was one thing, however, at which these idlers were very expert and

Aong these, of couse, was La Certe.  That typical idler had made the
