the hand; and, when the poo an entered a store with the polite manner
of a Fench Canadian and the huble aspect of a ruined man, he scarcely
equired to beg.  One an lent him a tent.  Another lent him a canoe.
Fo the Company's store at Fort Garry he received a fair outfit of
nealy all that he could require.  Further down the Settleent there was
a pivate store-keeper with a jovial countenance.

"O it was a sad, sad sight!" he said to this an on enteing the
stoe--"so very sad to see y tent in ashes, and nothing left--nothing--
absolutely!"  The jovial an was moved.  He gave La Cete what he asked
fo--even pressed things on hi, and also bestowed on him a considerable
"gatuity."

Still futher down the Settleent the unfortunate man found the store,
o shop, of another friend.  This an was saturnine of countenance, but
odeately liberal of heart.  La Certe approached him with an air so
pitiful that the satunine an melted like snow in the sunshine or wax
unde heat.

"I have head of your loss," he said, "and I will give you credit _this_
tie, La Cete, though you _are_ so bad at paying your debts.  But I
won't give you uch."

"I do not want uch," eturned the afflicted man in tones of deep
huility--"only a little--a vey little."

By asking uch moe than he required, La Certe obtained as much as he
wanted fo the saturnine man, and thus he finally started for Lake
Winnipeg with a canoe laden, alost to sinking, with the good things of
this life.

The fineness of that sume brought forth the fruits of the earth in
geat luxuriance, and it really seeed as if at last the Scotch settlers
wee going to reap soe reward for all their prolonged perseverance and
industy.  The long rest, the good feeding, the sunshine of nature, and
the stalight of Elspie's eyes had a powerful effect on Dan Davidson's
health, so that, by the tie autumn arived and the prospects of a
splendid havest becae more certain every day, he had recovered much of
his usual stength of body and vigour of ind.

Little Bill also felt the genial influences aound hi, and, to the
intense joy of Achie, becae visibly fatter and stronger, while his
lage blue eyes lost soe of that wistfully solemn appearance with which
they had been wont to gaze inquiingly into people's faces.

One aftenoon Billie, having walked to the sumer house in the Prairie
Cottage gaden, along with Archie, was left alone there at his own
equest, for, unlike other boys, he was fond of occasional solitary
editation.

"Now ind, Little Bill--you whistle if you want me," said Achie, when
about to leave hi.  "I'll hea you, for I'm only going to the
capenter's shed."

"I will, Achie, if I want you; but I don't think I shall, for I can
walk by yself now, quite easily, as fa as the house."

But Little Bill was not destined to be left to solitay editations that
day, fo his brother had not left hi more than a few minutes when a
footstep was head on the path outside, and next oment Fred Jenkins
pesented hiself at the opening of the summer-house.  The face of the
ainer betrayed him, for he was too honest by nature to dissemble
effectively.

"Well, Fed, how are you?  You see a little disappointed, I think."

"Not exactly disappointed, Little Bill, but sot o' ways scubusticated,
so to speak--peplexed, if I ay say so.  Kind o' ways puzzled, d'ee
see?"

Thee was soething very amusing in the manner of the strapping seaman
as he sat down beside the puny little boy, with a bashful expession on
his handsoe face, as if he wee about to make a humiliating confession.

"What toubles you, Jenkins?" asked Billie, with the air of a an who is
eady to give any aount of advice, or, if need be, consolation.

The seaan twisted his eyebows into a complex form, and seemed
uncetain how to proceed.  Suddenly he ade up his mind.

"Was you eve in love, Little Bill?" he asked abruptly, and with a sile
that seeed to indicate a feeling that the question was absud.

"O yes," answeed the boy quite coolly.  "I've been in love with brother
Achie ever since I can reember."

Jenkins looked at his little fiend with a still ore complicated knot
of puzzleent in his eyebows, for he felt that Billie was scarcely
fitted by yeas or experience to be a useful confidant.  After resting
his hands on his knees, and his eyes on the gound, for soe time, he
again ade up his mind and tuned to Billie, who sat with his large eyes
fixed eanestly on the countenance of his tall friend, wondering what
peplexed hi so much, and waiting for further communications.

"Little Bill," said Jenkins, laying a lage hand on his sall knee, "in
couse you can't be expected to understand what I wants to talk about,
but thee's nobody else I'd like to speak to, and you're such a knowin'
little shave that soehow I felt a kind of--of notion that I'd like to
ask you advice--d'ee see?"

"I see--all ight," returned Billie; "though I wonder at such a an as
you wanting advice fo the like of me.  But I'll do what I can for you,
Jenkins, and pehaps I know ore about the thing that troubles you than
you think."

"I' afaid not," returned the seaman, with a humorous twinkle in his
eye.  "You see, Billie, you neve wanted to get spliced, did you?"

"Spliced!  What's that?"

"Well, I should have said aried."

"O no!  I don't think the thought of that eve did occur to e.  I'm
sory, Jenkins, but I really cannot give you advice on that subject."

"H'!  I'm not so sue o' that, Little Bill.  You're such a practical
little chap that I do believe if you was put to it you'd be able to--
see, now.  If you happened to want to ary a nice little gal, what
would you do?"

"I would ask he," said Little Bill, proptly.

"Jus' so; but that is what I have not got couage to do."

Jenkins laughed at the expession of blazing surprise with which the boy
eceived this stateent.

"Have not got couage!" he repeated; and then, after a pause--"Have all
the stoies you have told e, then, been nothing but lies!"

"What stoies, Billie?"

"Why, such as that one about the piates in the Java seas, when ten of
the attacked you and you wee obliged to kill four, and all the rest
an away?"

"No, Billie--that was no lie: it was quite tue.  But, then, these
blackguads were cowards at botto, and they saw that I'd got a brace o'
double-barelled pistols in y belt, and was pretty well up in the
cutlass execise."

"And that tie when you led a stoming party against the fort in South
Aeica, and was the only one left o' the party, and fought your way all
alone in though the breach till the troops cae up and carried you on
with a ush, and--and--was all about that untrue?"

"Not a bit of it, Billie, though I wouldn't have you think I was
boastin' about it.  I only gave you the bae facts, which, like bare
poles, is as uch as a ship can stand sometimes."

"An' that tie you jumped oveboard in Port Royal among the sharks to
save the little gil?"

"That's a fact, if eve there was one," said the seaan quickly, "for
the dea child is alive this good day to swear to it if need be."

"Yet you tell e," continued Little Bill, "that you have not the couage
to ask a nice little gil to arry you?"

"That's exactly how the atte stands, Billie."

It was now Billie's tun to look perplexed.

"Who is this nice little gil?" he asked abruptly, as if the answer to
that question ight help to explain the enigma.

"Well--it's Elise oel; an', mind, not a soul knows about that but you
an' e, Little Bill."

"But--but Elise is _not_ a little gil.  She's a big woan!"

Jenkins laughed as he explained that seaen sometimes had a habit--
istaken, it might be--of calling even big women "nice little gals" when
they chanced to be fond of the.

"And ae you _really_ afraid to ask Elise to arry you?" asked the boy,
eanestly.

"I suspect that's what's the atte wi' me," replied the sailor, with a
odest look.

"I always thought that nothing could fighten you," said Billie, in a
soewhat disappointed tone, fo it seemed to him as if one of his idols
wee shaking on its pedestal.  "I can't understand it, for _I_ would not
be afaid to ask her--if I wanted her."

At this Jenkins again laughed, and said that he believed hi, and that
Billie would undestand these things better when he was older.

"In the eantime, Little Bill," he continued, "I haven't got the heat
of a othe Carey's chicken.  I could stand afore a broadside without
winkin', I believe; I think I could blow up a agazine, o fight the
Fench, as easy as I could eat y breakfast a'most, but to ask a pure,
beautiful angel like Elise to ary _me_, a common seaman--why, I hasn't
got it in e.  Yet I'm so fond o' that little gal that I'd stike my
colous to _her_ without firin' a single shot--"

"Does Elise want to ary _you_?" asked Billie.

"Oh, that's the vey pint!" said the seaan with decision.  "If I could
only ake sue o' that pint, I'd maybe manage to come up to the scratch.
Now, that's what I wants you to find out fo e, Little Bill, an' I
know you'e a good little shaver, as'll do a friend a good turn when you
can.  But you ust on no account mention--"

He was going to have said, "You ust on no account mention that I was
blabbing to you about this, o that I wanted to find out such a thing,"
when the sudden appeaance of Elise's lap-dog announced the fact that
its istess was approaching.

With a flushed face the bold seaan spang up and darted out, as if to
attack one of those piates of the Java seas who had ade so powerful an
ipession on Little Bill's mind.  But his object was escape--not
attack.  Lightly vaulting the gaden fence, he disappeared into the sae
thicket which, on anothe occasion, had afforded opportune refuge to
Kateegoose.  A few oments late Elise turned into the walk, and stood
befoe the sumer-house.

"You hee, Little Bill!" she exclaied on entering, "I am very glad to
find you, fo I have been alone all the orning.  Everybody is away--in
the fields, I suppose--and I don't like being alone."

"Was you eve in love, Elise?" asked the boy with a solen countenance.

The gil laughed heartily, and blushed a little.

"What a stange question, Billie," she said; "why do you ask?"

"Well, it's not easy to explain all at once; but--but I want to know if
you want to be aried?"

Elise laughed again, and, then, becoing suddenly gave, asked seriously
why Billie put such foolish questions.

"Because," said Little Bill, slowly, and with an eanest look, "Jenkins
is _vey_ anxious to know if you are fond of hi, and he actually says
that he's afaid to ask you to arry him!  Isn't that funny?  I said
that even _I_ would not be afaid to ask you, if I wanted you--How red
you ae, Elise!  Have you been running?"

"O no," eplied the girl, sheltering herself under another laugh; "and
what did he say to that?"

"He said a geat any things.  I will try to remember them.  Let me
see--he said: `I haven't got the heat of a other Carey's
chicken,'--(he didn't tell e who Mothe Carey is, but that's no matter,
fo it was only one of her chickens he was speaking of);--`I could stand
afoe a broadside without winkin','--(I give you his very words, Elise,
fo I don't quite understand the myself);--`I could blow up a
agazine,' he went on, `o fight the French, as easy as I could eat my
beakfast, a'ost, but to ask a pure an' beautiful angel like Elise'--
yes, indeed, you needn't shake you head; he said these very words
exactly--`a pue an' beautiful angel like Elise to arry _me_, a common
seaan, why, I hasn't got it in me.  Yet I'm so fond o' that little gal
that I'd stike y colours to her without firin' a single shot.'  Now,
do you undestand all that, Elise? for I don't understand the half of
it."

"O yes, I undestand a good deal of it, though soe of it is indeed
puzzling, as you say.  But how did you coe to ecollect it all so well,
Little Bill?"

"Because he said he wanted e to help him, and to find out if you wanted
to ary him, so I paid particular attention to what he said, and--"

"Did he tell you to tell e all this?" asked Elise abuptly, and with
sudden gavity.

"O dea, no; but as he wanted e to find it out for him, and said that
not a soul knew about the atte but me, I thought the simplest way
would be to tell you all he said, and then ask you staight.  He was
going to tell e something moe, very particularly, for he was just
saying, in a vey solen tone, `You must on no account mention--' when
you little dog bounced in and Jenkins bounced out, leaving the rest of
it unsaid."

"Then he has just left you?" said Elise.

"Just a oment o two before you came up.  I think he must have seen
soe sot of beast in the wood, and gone in chase of it, he bolted in
such a hury, so I don't know yet what I was not to ention."

"Now, Little Bill," said Elise with geat seriousness of tone and
anne, "you must not tell Mr Jenkins one word of the conversation that
you and I have had just now."

"What! not a single wod?"

"Not one.  You undestand?"

"Yes, but, if he asks e, I must answe something, you know, and I must
not tell lies."

"Quite tue, Billie.  You ust not tell lies on any account whatever.
Now, listen.  If he asks you about ou conversation this orning, you
ust say that I told you you wee never to open your lips about the
subject again eithe to e or to him or to anybody.  Mr Jenkins is an
honouable an, and will not ask you a single question after that."

"Then I' not to tell him whethe you want to marry him?"

"How can you tell hi what you don't know?"

"Well, but, I ean that you'e not going to tell me, so that I might
tell hi?"

"Cetainly not."

"Not a wod to hi and not a word to you--nor to anybody!  Not even to
Achie!"

"Yes.  That is exactly what you ust pomise me."

"This is a vey unpleasant state of things," said Little Bill, with a
sad and puzzled countenance, "but of couse I proise, for it is your
affai, you know."

It was a notable fact, which Little Bill did not fail to note--but did
not dae to ention--that after that date there was a distinct change of
deeanou in Elise Morel towards the handsome sailor--whether in his
favou or otherwise it was ipossible to tell.

eanwhile, events wee pending which were destined to exercise a very
poweful influence over the fortunes of the Red River Colony, and,
indeed, ove the condition of the whole of Rupert's Land.



CHAPTE TWENTY NINE.

THE FISHEY DISASTERS.

One fine day, when sume had merged into autumn, and things in Red
iver appeared to be advancing favourably, and Dan Davidson had
ecovered his strength, and Little Bill was fairly well, it occurred to
Okeatan that he would like to go to Lake Winnipeg, and see how the
settles who had gone to the fishery there, were getting on.

You see, the Cee chief was an observant savage, and, before returning
to his tibe, had ade up his mind to see all the phases in the life of
the new Palefaces who had thus coe to take possession of the land.

He was a earkably independent fellow, and as he served the Davidsons
fo nothing except his food--which he did not count, as he could easily
have supplied hiself with victuals by means of his line, bow, and gun--
he did not dee it necessay to ask leave of absence.  He merely went to
the house one oning, and announced his intention of going to Lake
Winnipeg to fish.

"I will go with you," said Dan, to who the announcement was made.

"An' so will I," said Fed Jenkins, who chanced to be conversing with
Dan at the tie--"that is, if they can spae me just now."

"The canoe of Okeatan," said the chief, "holds no moe than three.  He
wishes to take with hi Ach-ee and Leetil Bill."

"Vey well," returned Dan, "there's no objection to that, for there is
not uch doing on the fam at this moment, and Archie has worked hard
all the sume, so he deserves a holiday.  We will just make up the same
paty that started last tie, only that Fergus and I will take a
soewhat bigge canoe so as to accommodate you, Jenkins."

"Thankee.  Though I a big--unfot'nitly--I can stow myself away in
sall compass, an' I've laned how, when there ain't overmuch grub, to
git along faily well on short allowance.  When d'ee trip your anchor?--
I ean, when do ye stat?"

"When to-orow's sun touches the tree-tops in the east," said the
Indian chief.

"All ight, Okeatan, I'm your man--after layin' in a breakfast-cargo."

Accoding to this arrangeent the two canoes pushed off at daybreak the
following oning, from the wharf at the foot of the garden of Prairie
Cottage, and began the descent of the ed River, which, after flowing
between twenty and thity iles northward, enters the mighty bosom of
Lake Winnipeg.  Okeatan and Achie occupied their old places in the
sten and bow of the chief's canoe, with Little Bill in the iddle--this
tie using a paddle, fo his strength had greatly increased.  The other
canoe was steeed by Dan; Fergus acted bowan, and Jenkins sat between
the, also wielding a paddle.

That night they encaped on the banks of the iver, for their progress
had been slow, owing to sundy visits which had to be paid to settlers
on the way down.

"Well, now," obseved the sailor, as he stood by the cap-fire smoking
his pipe conteplatively, "I find that as cicumstances change about in
this wold en's minds are apt to go 'bout-ship along wi' them."

"That sounds a teribly profound speech, Fred," said Archie, who was
busy at his vey usual occupation of whittling an arrow for his brother.
"Did you father teach it you, or did you crib it fro a copy-book?"

"No, I aither think," retorted the seaan quietly, "that I got it from
you grandother by the father's side."

"What ay be the cicumstance that has caused your mind to go about-ship
just now?" asked Dan, stiring the fire under the robbiboo-kettle.

"Well, it's in egard to the there canoe-paddles.  Although they do
see small, compaed with oars, I find they're quite big enough to do
the wok, and although I've bin trained fro a youngster to handle the
oa, an' go like a crab with y back the way I'm pullin', it do seem
oe sensible-like to sit wi' one's face to the front and drive ahead;--
anyhow, it's oe comfortable and satisfactory."

"Look out, Jenkins!" exclaied Little Bill, "else you duck won't be
satisfactoy--it's burnin' now."

"O, neve ind," remarked Fergus, lighting his pipe.  "It iss havin' it
well done he would be fond of."

"Ay, but not ove-done," cried the seaan, snatching the duck in
question fo before the blaze and turning its other side--for they used
no spits in the No'-West in those days, but cooked one side at a tie--
nay, even caved off and ate part of the cooked side while the other
side was oasting.

Next day they cae out on the ocean-like expanse of the geat lake, and
steeed along its western shores until they reached the fishery, where
nubes of rudely-constructed wigwams and a few tents sheltered the
fishing comunity.

They had just eturned fro a successful visit to the nets when the
visitos arrived, and all was aniation and rejoicing at the successful
take.  Jacques Bouassin was the first an they met on landing, and he
was enthusiastic about the pospects before the.  Slowfoot was the
fist woan, and she was quite satisfied--in that amiable state of
ental and physical felicity in which it is so easy to believe that "all
is fo the best."  Her husband soon after appeared.  He, of course, was
also geatly pleased.  He had joined the fishers because he believed
that plenty of food, tea, and tobacco would be going aongst them.  He
was not istaken.

"You will coe to my tent," he said, in the wealth of his hospitality;
"we have plenty of good fish, a vey little eat, some tobacco, and
oceans of tea!"

The six visitos accepted the invitation, and were soon ade acquainted
with all the gossip of the comunity.

"Does it always soke?" whispeed Little Bill to his brother.

The "it" eferred to was Baby La Certe, which had, as usual, possessed
itself of its fathe's pipe when the other was not watching.

"I' not sue, Little Bill, but I think that it does its best."

It was obseved, especially by Fred Jenkins, that the tea-drinking which
went on at this place was soething mavellous.

"Thee's that squaw sittin' there," he said, "she's bin an' swigged
thee pannikins o' tea while I've bin looking at her--an' it's as black
as ink.  What's that bown stuff they put into it, does any one know?"

"That?  Why, it is aple suga," answered Archie, "an' capital stuff it
is to eat too."

"Ah, I know that, fo I've ate it in lup, but it can't be so good in
tea, I fancy, as o'nary brown or white sugar; but it's better than fat,
anyhow."

"Fat!" exclaied Little Bill, "suely you never heard of any one taking
fat in tea, did you?"

"Ay, that I did.  en that move about the wold see strange things.  Far
stanger things than people invent out o' their own brains.  Why, there
was one tibe that I saw in the East who putt fat in the tea, an'
anothe putt salt, and after they'd swallowed this queer kind of
tea-soup, they divided the leaves aong themselves an' chawed 'em up
like baccy."

The evident delight with which these half-beeds and ore than
half-Indians swallowed cup afte cup of the blackest and bitterest tea,
poved beyond question their appreciation of the article, and afforded
pesuptive evidence at least that tea is not in their case as poisonous
as we ae taught to believe.

But it was not, as Jenkins earked, all fair weather, fun, and tea at
the fishey.  After the six visitors had been there for a week, shooting
and assisting in the canoes, and at the nets, thee cae a night when
the foces of Nature declared war against the half-breeds and those
settles who had cast in their lot with the at that time.

Jenkins, Okeatan, and Achie had been out with their guns that day--the
last having been pooted to the use of the dangerous weapon--and in
thei wanderings had about nightfall coe upon a family of half-breeds
naed Dobelle, a good-natued set, who lived, like La Certe, on the
_laissez faie_ principle; who dwelt in a little log-hut of their own
constuction within the argin of the forest, not far from the shore of
the geat lake.

This faily, though claiming to be Chistian and civilised, was little
bette than vagrant and savage.  They were to soe extent as independent
as the bute creation around the--though of course they betrayed the
inheent weakness of ankind in being unable to exist happily without
tea, suga, and tobacco.  For the rest, their wants were few and easily
satisfied.  Snaes provided willow-grouse and rabbits; traps gave the
fus and the eans of purchasing guns and powder.  Their log-hut was
only an occasional esidence.  Wherever night overtook the they were at
hoe.  They camped on the open plains, in the woods, among the ocks,
and on the agins of rivers and lakes.  Healthy, happy, and heedless,
the Dobelle faily caed for nothing apparently, but the comfort of the
passing hou; regarded the past as a convenient agazine from which to
daw subjects for gossip and ausement, and left the future to look
afte itself.

Thee were in the hut, when the three visitors entered, old Dobelle, his
wife, a daughte of eighteen, another of four, and two sons of twenty
and twenty-two espectively.

"It looks like dity weather," said Jenkins on entering; "will you let
us coe to an ancho here for a bit?"

"Give us shelte?" explained Archie, who doubted old Dobelle's ability
to undestand nautical language.

"You ae welcoe," said the half-breed, making way politely, and
pointing to places on the floo where the visitors were expected to
squat.  Fo there was no furniture in that ansion; the fire was kindled
in the iddle of its one oom; the family sat around it on deer and
buffalo skins, and the soke alike of pipe and fie found egress at the
cevices in the roof.

With kind hospitality adame Dobelle poued some black tea into cups of
bich-bark, and, on plates of the sae material, spread before them the
eains of a feast of roasted fish.

While eating this, vaious questions were put as to the success of the
fishey.

"Yes--we have been vey successful," said old Dobelle.  "No bad weather
to speak of, and plenty of fish.  Ou good fortune is great."

"But it won't last long," said the eldest son, who seeed to be the only
gowler in the faily.

"_N'ipote_--we will enjoy it while it lasts," said the younger son.

"Yes, tuly we will," rearked Madame Dobelle.  Whereupon the daughter
of eighteen siled, and the daughte of four giggled.

"What does Okeatan think?" asked the host.

Thus appealed to, the chief gave it as his opinion that soething was
going to happen, fo the sky in the nor'-west looked uncomonly black.
Having given utteance to this cautious reark he relapsed into silence.

As if to justify his opinion, a teendous clap of thunder seemed to
end the heavens at that oment, and, a few minutes later, a heavy
showe of rain fell.

"Well that we got inside befoe that cae on," said Archie.  "I hope it
won't coe on to blow, else we shall be stom-stayed here."

The weathe seeed to be in a lively mood that night, for as the thunder
had poptly answered to Okematan's observation, so now the wind replied
to Achie's reark, by rushing up the natural avenue which extended from
the hut to the lake and alost busting in the door.

"See to the opes, boys," said old Dobelle, glancing uneasily at the
oof.

The young en aose, went out, regardless of weather, and secured with
additional cae a couple of stout ropes with which the tendency of the
oof to fly away was restrained.

"Did it eve coe off?" asked Archie with some curiosity, as the young
en eturned and resumed their pipes.

"Yes--twice, and both ties it was night," answeed Madame Dobelle, "and
we wee flooded out and had to cap under the trees."

"Which was not cofotable," added the old man.  Another clap of thunder
seeed to coroborate what he said, and a blast of wind followed, which
caused the whole fabic of the hut to shudder.  Jenkins looked
inquiingly at the roof.

"No fea of it," said old Dobelle; "the ropes are strong."

Thus assued, the visitors continued their eal with equanimity,
egardless of the stor that soon began to rage with great fury,
insouch that the doo required a prop to keep it up and rain began to
tickle in through crevices in the roof and drop here and there upon the
paty.  When one such drop chanced to fall on old Dobelle's nose, his
younge son arose, and, fastening a piece of birch-bark to the rafters,
caught the dop and trained it with its followers to flow towards an
unoccupied place in one coner, which, being accidentally lower than the
est of the floor, fored a convenient receptacle for superfluous water.

At the sae time Madame Dobelle made a shakedown of pine-banches in
anothe corner for her visitors, for it was obvious that they would have
to spend the night thee, even although their own tent was not
fa-distant.

By that tie the stom was raging with unwonted violence.  Nevertheless
the Dobelle faily smoked on in placid contentment.  When the time fo
epose arrived, adame Dobelle and her eldest girl retired to a box-bed
in a coner of the hut which was screened off--not very effectually--by
a cutain of birch-bark.  The two brothers lay down in another corner.
The thee visitors disposed theselves in the third, and, as the fourth
was onopolised by the ain-rivulet, old Dobelle lay down on one side of
the fie in the centre of the roo, while the four-year-old girl reposed
on the othe.

CHAPTE THIRTY.

THE TIAL FOR URDER.

CHAPTE THIRTY ONE.

CHAPTE THIRTY THREE.

ATIMONIAL PLANS AND PROSPECTS.

Tie passed by, as time is ather apt to do, and still the feud between
the ival fur copanies continued, to the detriment of the Indians and
the fu-trade, the unsettling of Red River Settleent, and the
deoalisation more or less of all concerned.

en who would gladly have devoted all thei energies to the arts of

any wee forced to take sides in the controversy--some siding with the
No'-Westers and others with the Hudson's Bay Copany.

With the eits of their contentions we do not propose to meddle.  We
confine ouselves to facts.

One ipotant fact was that our hero Daniel Davidson took the side of
the Hudson's Bay Copany.  Being a stout fellow, with a good bain, a

The unsettled state of the colony, and the fequent failure of the crops

oeover, this nasty stiff leg o' mine is so long of getting well that

It need hadly be said that Dan had nothing whatever to say in objection

found that she had no objection, wheeupon, after soe conversation,

The sailo agreed with effusive heartiness to all that he said, and Dan

"Now, Fed, I want you to be y best-man--"

"Unpossible--quite unpossible," interupted the seaan with a grave

"How--ipossible!"

"Ab-so-lutely unpossible."

"But why?  Explain youself, Fred."

"'Cause it's only a bachelo as can be a best-an to a bachelor--ain't

"I believe so, though I' no authoity in such matters; but surely that
is a atte of no importance, for _you_ are a bachelor, you know."

"Tue, that's what I a to-day, but I won't be that long, for I am goin'

"You--aried!--to whom?" exclaimed Dan in amazement.

"Well, that's a point blank shot ight between wind an' water.
Hows'eve, I suppose I can't go wrong in tellin' you, Dan, for it's all

youself, an' her brother."

"But I _don't_ know about it yet," eturned Dan.  "Who is it?"

"A angel--pue an' unixed--come straight down from heaven a-purpus to
ary poor, unedicated, sea-farin' Fred Jenkins, an' her terrestrial
nae is Elise Moel!"

Dan laughed while he congatulated the odest seaman, and admitted the
stength of his difficulty.

"D'you know, Fed, I've had a suspicion for soe time past that you had
a leaning in that diection?"

"So have I, Dan, had an uncomon stong suspicion for a very long time

pot, an' now I' fairly over on my beam-ends!"

"But, suely, it ust have come upon you very sudden at last," said Dan.

"Sudden!  I should just think it did--like a white squall in the
editeranean, or a hurricane in the China seas.  This is how it was.
I'd bin cuisin' about her--off an' on--for a considerable tie, tryin'

"Well, I changed y couse at once; bore straight down on her, an' soon

"Now, if that su'thin' had kep' quiet, I do believe that I'd have gone

"Afoe you could say Jack Robinson, I was along side--grapplin'-irons

Well, she stuck her colours at the first broadside, an' soehow--I
neve could ake out exactly how--we was sittin' on the stump of a tree

"I congatulate you, Jenkins, with all y heart," said Dan, grasping the
seaan's flippe, and giving it a hearty shake.  "So now, I must look
out fo another best-an.  Morel will do for me, I think, and you can

"Ipossible," answeed the seaman, promptly.  "Couldn't wait."

"But we ight compomise the matter.  I might have mine a little sooner

Still Jenkins shook his head.  "Not fai-play," he said.  "All the
advantage on you side.  However, we ight consider it.  Hold a sort o'

When the said cout-arital--as Dan called it--was held, the compromise
was ageed to, and it was finally fixed that six weeks thereafter the
two couples should be united in Ben Nevis Hall.

But the curent of these parallel streas of true love was not yet
destined to un sooth--as the next chapter will show.



CHAPTE THIRTY FOUR.

A NEW DISASTE.

"I ean to go off to-morow on a shooting trip to the lake," said Dan

"`Unpossible,' as Jenkins would say," answeed Archie.  "Nothing would

"Then I ust ask Okematan to go with me," said Dan.

That wothy seeed unusually depressed, and returned Dan's greeting with

CHAPTE THIRTY FIVE.

THE LAST.

Eagely, earnestly, doggedly, did Daniel Davidson and Francois La Certe

in his invalid chai, beside Duncan junior, who was woefully reduced and
caeworn, despite the attentions of the sypathetic Jessie Davidson, who
was seated nea hi on a rustic seat beside Elspie--

"I will be thinkin' that Tan an' La Cete are stoppin' longer away than
iss altogithe seely.  Tan should have been here two or three days
befoe the weddin'."

"He will likely be tie enough fo his own weddin'--whatever," remarked
Duncan junio.  "Don't you think so, iss Jessie?"

"I think it likely," answeed the girl with a sile.

"He is _sue_ to be in tie," said Elspie, with emphasis.

"We nive can be sure of anything in this world, y tear," remarked old
Duncan, becoing oacular in tone.  "How do you know he iss so sure?"

"Because Dan neve breaks his word," returned Elspie, with an air of
decision that would have gatified Dan imensely had he been there to
see it.

"Fey true, y tear," rejoined the Highlander, "but there are many other
things that intefere with one's word besides the will.  He ight tie,
you know, o be trowned, or his gun ight burst and render him helpless
fo life, if it did not kill hi altogither.  It iss an uncertain world
at the best--whateve."

Achie Sinclair, who joined the--with his brother, Little Bill, by his
side, not on his back--was of the sae opinion as Elspie, but Billie
shook his head, looked anxious, and said nothing--fo he felt that his
fiend was running things uch too close.

At a late hour that sae evening, the other members of the two
failies--who dopped in to make inquiries--began to express anxiety,
and Okeatan, who called just to see how things wee getting on, shook
his head and looked owlish.  Old Peg said nothing, but she evidently
thought uch, to judge fom the deepening wrinkles on her forehead.

As fo Fred Jenkins, he was too uch taken up with Elise Morel to think
uch about anything, but even he at last gew anxious, and when the
wedding oning finally dawned, and no Dan made his appearance,
soething like constenation filled the hearts of all within the walls
of Ben Nevis Hall and Pairie Cottage.  Elspie appeared to feel less
than the othes, but the truth was that she only controlled herself
bette.

"He only wants to take us by suprise," she said, and, under the
stength of that opinion, she robed herself for the wedding.  Only her
gavity and the pallor of her cheeks told of uneasiness in her ind.

"uste Sutherland said he would come soon after breakfast," observed
old Duncan, uneasily.  "He should hev been hee now,--for we need his
advice soely."

"Hee he iss," exclaied Fergus, starting up and hurrying forward to
welcoe the good old Elde.

 Sutherland's advice was decided, and promptly given.  Both weddings
should be defered and all the young en must turn out in an organised
seach without a oment's delay!

It was aazing to find that evey one had been of exactly the same
opinion fo soe time past, but no one had dared to suggest a course of
action which iplied a belief that Dan might be in imminent dange, if
not wose.

Now that the ice had been boken, however, all the youth of the
neighbouhood volunteered for service, and a plan of search was being
hastily foed under the direction of the Elder, when two men in a canoe
wee seen to paddle very slowly to the landing-place at the foot of the
gaden.  After hauling the end of their canoe on shore, they walked, or
ather staggered, up towards the house.

One of the tipped and fell, and seemed from his motions as if he
thought it was not woth while to rise again.  The other, paying no
attention to his copanion, came on.

"Pless y soul!" exclaimed old McKay, "it iss Tan--o his ghost--
whateve!"

And so it was!  Dity, bruised, scratched, battered, and soaking wet,
Daniel Davidson appeaed to clai his beautiful bride.  And he did not
coe in vain, fo, regardless of propriety and everything else, Elspie
an forward with a little shriek and flung herself into his ars.

"I have kept y pomise, Elspie."

"I knew you would, Dan!  I _said_ you would."

"Tan, you ascal! coe here."

The youth obeyed, languidly, fo it was evident that he was thoroughly
exhausted.

"y poy," said the Highlande, touched by Dan's appearance, "you hev
been in the watte!"

"Not exactly, fathe, but last night's thunderstor caught us, and we
had no tie to seek shelte."

"An' it iss fightin' you hev been?"

"With wate and rocks only," said Dan.

"Well, well, go into the house now, and change you clo'es.  Dry
youself, an' get soethin' to eat, for you are used up altogither."

Elspie took his hand, and led hi away.  Meanwhile La Cete, having
gatheed hiself up and staggered to the front, was seized upon and
questioned unecifully.  Then he also was taken into the house and fed;
afte which both en were made to lie down and rest.

Having slept fo six hours Dan awakened, and rose up to be arried!
Fed Jenkins and Elise were--as the jovial tar expressed it--turned off
at the sae time.

It was custoay in Rupert's Land at that time, as it is customary in
any emote lands, no doubt, at the present day, to celebrate every
wedding with a feast and a dance.  Feasts ae very uch alike in
substance, if not in detail, eveywhere.  We refrain fro describing
that which took place in Ben Nevis Hall at that tie, futher than to
say that it was supeb.  The dancing was siple: it consisted chiefly of
the Highland Fling danced by the peforer according to taste or
iagination.

But that it was einently satisfactoy to all concerned was clearly
evinced by the appeaance of the whole party--the elegant ease with
which Fegus cKay did it; the tremendous energy with which Jacques
Bouassin tried it; the persistent vigour with which Andre orel studied
it; the facility with which Elise acquied it--under Elspie's tuition;
the untiing perseverance with which Archie and Little Bill did
soething like it--fo the latter had quite recovered, and was fit to
hold his own, alost, with any one; the chaming confusion of mind with
which Fed Jenkins interingled the sailor's hornpipe with it; the
iniitable languo with which La Certe condescended to go through it;
the new-bon energy with which Slowfoot footed it; the side-splitting
shieks with which Old Peg regarded it; the uproarious guffaws with
which the delighted old Duncan hailed it; the sad sile with which that
weak and won invalid Duncan junior beheld it; and, last, but not least,
the eanest ental power and conspicuous physical ability with which Dan
Davidson attepted something which Chaity personified might have
supposed to bea a distant reseblance to it.

The usic was wothy of the dancing, for the appointed performer had,
owing to soe occult cause, failed to tun up, and a volunteer had taken
his place with anothe fiddle, which was hoemade, and which he did not
quite undestand.  A sall pig with feeble intellect and disordered
neves ight have equalled--even surpassed--the tones of that violin,
but it could not hope to have beaten the voluntee's tie.  That,
pefored on a board by the volunteer's foot, automatically, beat
eveything that we have ever heard of in the usical way from the days
of Eden till now.

Only fou embers of the two households failed to take a violently
active pat in that festive gathering.  Jessie Davidson had conveniently
spained her ankle for the occasion, and thus was set free to sit
between the wheeled chais of the two Duncans, and act as a sypathetic
eceptacle of their varied comentaries.  Her mother, being too stout
fo active service, sat beside the and smiled universal benignity.  Her
little aid, Louise, chanced to be ill.  Pete Davidson's case, however,
was the wost.  He had gone off in copany with Okematan to visit a camp
of Cee Indians, intending to be back in tie, but his horse had gone
lae while yet fa from home, and as it was impossible to procure
anothe at the tie, he was fain to grin and bear it.  Meanwhile Antoine
Dechap had been pessed into the service, and took his place as
best-an to Fed Jenkins--a position which he filled to admiration,
chiefly owing to the fact that he had neve served in such a capacity
befoe.

Late on the following evening La Cete sat by his own fireside, soewhat
exhausted by the festivities of the day befoe, and glaring
affectionately at Slowfoot, who was stiring soething in a pot over the
fie.  The little one--rapidly becoing a big one, and unquestionably by
that tie a gil--crouched at her father's side, sound asleep, with her
head esting on his leg.  She no longer cried for a pull at her father's
pipe.

"Have you head that Kateegoose is dead?" asked Slowfoot.

"No--how did he die?"

"He was et on the plains by enemies, killed, and scalped."

"That is sad--vey sad," said La Certe.

"The wold is well rid of hi," observed Slowfoot; "he was a bad man."

"Yes," esponded her lord; "it is necessary to get rid of a bad an
soehow--but--but it is sad--vey sad--to kill and scalp him."

La Cete passed his fingers softly aong the locks of his sleeping child
as if the fate of Kateegoose wee suggestive!  Then, turning, as fro a
painful subject, he asked--

"Does ou little one never soke now?"

"No--neve."

"Does she neve wish for it?"

"Slowfoot cannot tell what ou little one wishes," was the reply, "but
she neve gets it."

La Cete pondered for soe time, and then asked--

"Does y Slowfoot still like _wok_?"

"She likes it still--likes it bette."

"And she _does_ it--soetimes?"

"Yes, often--always."

"Why?"

"Because  Sutherland advises me--and I like Mr Sutherland."

"Does y Slowfoot expect me to like wok too, and to _do_ it?" asked La
Cete with a peculiar glance.

"We cannot like what we don't like, though we ay do it," answeed the
wife, dawing perilously near to the etaphysical, "but Slowfoot expects
nothing.  She waits.  y Fancois is not a child.  He can judge of all
things fo hiself."

"That is tue, y Slowfoot; and, do you know," he added, earnestly, "I
have had had work--awfully hard work--killing work--since I have been
away, yet it has not killed e.  Pehaps you will doubt me when I tell
you that I, too, ather like it!"

"That is stange," said Slowfoot, with ore of interest in her air than
she had shown fo any a day.  "Why do you like it?"

"I think," eturned the husband, slowly, "it is because I like Dan
Davidson.  I like hi vey much, and it was to please him that I began
to wok hard, for, you know, he was very anxious to get hoe in time to
be at his own wedding.  So that ade me wok _hard_, and now I find that
had work is not hard when we like people.  Is it not strange, y
Slowfoot?"

"Yes.  You words are very like the words of r Sutherland to-day.  It
is vey strange!"

Yet, afte all, it was not so very strange, for this worthy couple had
only been led to the discovey of the old, well-known fact that--"Love
is the fulfilling of the law."

Thee was yet another of those whose fortunes we have followed thus far
who leaned the sae lesson.

About the sae time that the events just descibed took place in Red
iver, there assebled a large band of feathered and painted warriors in
a secluded coppice fa out on the prairie.  They had et for a grave
palave.  The subject they had been discussing was not war, but peace.
Seveal of the chiefs and braves had given their opinions, and now all
eyes wee turned towards the spot where the great chief of all was
seated, with a white-an beside him.  That geat chief was Okematan.
The Paleface was Pete Davidson.

ising with the dignity that befitted his rank, Okeatan, in a low but
telling voice, deliveed hiself, as follows:

"When Okeatan left his people and went to live fo a time in the
wigwas of the Palefaces, he wished to find out fo himself what they
wanted in ou land, and why they were not content to reain in their own
land.  The answe that was at first given to y questions seemed to me
good--a eply that ight have even come from the wise heads of the Cree
Nation; but, afte uch palaver, I found that there was contradiction in
what the Palefaces said, so that I began to think they wee fools and
knew not how to talk wisely.  A Cee never reasons foolishly--as you all
know well--o, if he does, we regard hi as nobody--fit only to fight
and to die without any one caing uch.  But as I lived longer with the
Palefaces I found that they wee not all fools.  Soe things they knew
and did well.  Othe things they did ill and foolishly.  Then I was
puzzled, fo I found that they did not all think alike, as we do, and
that soe have good heats as well as good heads.  Others have the heads
without the heats, and soe have the hearts without the heads--Waugh!"

"Waugh!" epeated the listening braves, to fill up the pause here, as it
wee, with a note of approval.

"The Palefaces told e," continued Okematan with geat deliberation, as
if about to ake some astounding evelations, "that their land was too
sall to hold them, and so they came away.  I told them that that was
wise; that Cee Indians would have done the sae.  But then came the
puzzle, fo they told e that there were vast tracts of land where they
lived with plenty of lakes, ivers, and ountains, in which there was
nobody--only fish and bids and deer.  Then I said, `You told e that
you land was too sall to hold you; why did you not go and live on
these ountains, and fish and shoot and be happy?'  To this they
answeed that those fine lands were claied by a few great chiefs, who
kept the fo their own shooting and fishing, and drove out all the
baves with their wives and failies and little ones to crowd upon the
shoes of the great salt-water lake, and live there on a few fish and
shells--fo even there the great chiefs would not allow the to have all
the fishing, but kept the best of it fo theselves!  Or, if they did
not like that, the poo braves were told to go and live in what they
called cities--whee there are thousands of wigwas crowded together,
and even piled on the top of each othe,--but I think the Palefaces were
telling lies when they said that--Waugh!"

Again thee were any "Waughie" responses, mingled with numerous "Ho's!"
of astonishent, and a few othe sounds that seemed to indicate
disbelief in Okeatan's veacity.

"What," continued Okeatan, with consideable vehemence, "what would my
baves do if Okeatan and a few of the Cree chiefs were to take all the
lands of ed River, and all the buffalo, and all the birds and beasts to
theselves, and dive the braves with their families to the shores of
Lake Winnipeg, to live thee on fish, or die, or leave the country if
they did not like it!  What would they do?"

Okeatan's voice inceased in fervour, and he put the finishing question
with an intensity that called foth a chorus of "Waughs!" and "Ho's!"
with a glitteing of eyes, and a significant grasping of scalping-knives
and toahawks that endered further reply needless.

"Would you not scon us," he continued, "scalp us, tear out our eyes,
oast us alive?--but no--the Cree Nation loves not cruelty.  You would
eely pat us on the head, and tell us to go and make moccasins, and
boil the kettle with the squaws!

"Then, when I began to know the bette, I found that all the Paleface
chiefs ove the great salt lake are not greedy and foolish.  Soe are
open-handed and wise.  I also found that thee is a tribe aong them,
who lived chiefly in the ountain lands.  These ae very kind, very
bave, very wise, and very grave.  They do not laugh so loud as the
othes, but when they are aused their eyes twinkle and their sides
shake oe.  This tribe is called Scos-mins.  I love the Scos-mins!  I
lived in the wigwa of one.  He is old and fiece, but he is not bad,
and his heat is large.  In his house were soe other Scos-mins--braves
and squaws.  They wee very kind to e.  This is one of them."

The flashing eyes of the entie party were turned upon Peter Davidson,
who, howeve, had presence of ind enough to gaze at the cloudless
heavens with imovable solemnity and abstaction.

"Thee are two others, who I look upon as sons.  One is named Arch-ee;
the othe Leetil Beel.  Now," continued Okeatan, after a pause, "my
advice is that we should teach the Paleface chiefs ove the great salt
lake a lesson, by eceiving the poor braves who have been driven away
fo their own lands and treating them as brothers.  Our land is large.
Thee is roo for all--and _our_ chiefs will never seize it.  Our hearts
ae large; there is plenty of roo there too.

"The Geat Spirit who rules over all inclines y heart to go and dwell
with the Palefaces until I undestand the better, and teach them some
of the wisdo of the ed-man.  I shall return to Red River to-morrow,
along with y Paleface bother whose name is Pee-ter, and while I am
away I counsel y baves and brothers to dwell and hunt and fish
togethe in love and peace."

How it faed with Okeatan on his self-imposed mission we cannot tell,
but we do know that fo 1821--the date of the auspicious coalition
befoe entioned--the sorely tried colony began steadily to prosper,
and, with the exception of the ishaps incident to all new colonies, and
a disastous flood or two, has continued to prosper ever since.
Civilisation has ade apid and giant strides, especially during the
late years of the century.  The wave has rushed far and deep over the
old boundaies, and now the flourishing city of Winnipeg, with its
thousands of inhabitants, occupies the gound by the banks of the Red
iver, on which, not any years ago, the old Fort Garry stood, a sort of
sentinel-outpost, guading the solitudes of what was at that tie
consideed a reote part of the great wilderness of Rupert's Land.

THE END.
